LEADER
 Big data  begins to flex its lobbying muscles

610 words
18 October 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
08
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Silicon Valley's loss of innocence in politics highlights need for reform

It used to be said that Wall Street held the swing vote in US money politics. Defence and energy always plumped for Republicans while Hollywood and Silicon Valley were reliably Democratic. That is no longer entirely true.

With less than three weeks before the US midterm elections, big data companies are hedging their bets. More than half of Google's donations have gone to Republicans. The company has overtaken Goldman Sachs as the largest corporate donor in these elections.

Moreover, the US tech sector has shed its distaste for lobbying. Google's "Don't be evil" mantra signalled disdain for the business of politics. Nowadays it is heavily represented in Washington, Brussels and elsewhere.

Alongside oil and defence groups, big data is taking its place. Internet utopians and techie libertarians may decry Silicon Valley's loss of innocence. But businesses tend to respond to the world as it is rather than how it should be.

Big data's growing comfort with money politics is partly an acknowledgment of political reality. Republicans will almost certainly increase their majority in the House of Representatives next month and may well gain control of the Senate. That will enable them to set the agenda on issues that affect Silicon Valley's bottom line, such as skilled immigrant visas, research and development budgets and the National Security Agency's ability to snoop on their customers' data.

These are legitimate high-tech concerns. But companies such as Apple and Google are also teaming up with other corporations, notably in the pharmaceutical sector, to lobby for tax holidays on repatriated overseas profits. There is nothing idealistic about this. Nor is there anything nefarious. Those with high intellectual property content, such as big data, find it easier to shift profits to more lightly taxed jurisdictions via transfer pricing. It is the job of tax authorities to tighten up rules to stem large-scale avoidance. Companies in all sectors try to maximise shareholder return. "Don't be naive", should be Google's new motto.

That said, US politics is awash with corporate money and big data plays an important role. Last year, Facebook's Mark Zuckerberg teamed up with counterparts at Microsoft, Google and others to create Fwd.Us, a Silicon Valley advocacy group with $50m in start-up cash.

Its purpose is to pressure lawmakers to pass immigration reform and other overdue bills. In practice, however, it is like any other moneyed advocate: funnelling money to pliable candidates and funding attack advertisements. Facebook and Google have set up political action committees - vehicles that magnify their influence on legislators.

Unsurprisingly, the public is more disaffected than ever with politics as usual. Approval ratings for Congress are at an all-time low. In contrast, the well-heeled denizens of "K Street" - the stretch of Washington where lobby firms are concentrated - have rarely squeezed such high returns out of Capitol Hill.

Silicon Valley firms are among K Street's most favoured clients. Politicians of all shades are lining up for tech industry handouts. Yet it makes no sense to single out big data for opprobrium. The US is crying out for sensible corporate tax reform. It also desperately needs a better way of regulating the role of money in politics. Neither problem originated with big data.

Companies such as Google are simply exploiting the loopholes available to fatten their returns and protect their interests. That is what companies do. It is for politicians to legislate in the public interest. Sadly, the reality in Washington is far removed from that ideal.

US
Mortgage Giants Set to Loosen Lending; Fannie, Freddie Near Deal to Lift Limits; Concerns Persist

By Joe Light 
1,058 words
17 October 2014
19:56
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Fannie Mae, Freddie Mac and mortgage lenders are nearing an agreement that could lower barriers and restrictions on borrowers with weak credit, a move that would expand access to home loans amid the sluggish housing recovery.

The move by the mortgage-finance giants and their regulator, the Federal Housing Finance Agency, would help lenders protect themselves from claims of making bad loans, according to people familiar with the matter.

Fannie and Freddie are also considering programs that would make it easier for lenders to offer mortgages with down payments of as little as 3% for some borrowers, the people said. That would be a reversal for the loan giants. The moves could be announced as soon as this coming week.

Regulators and White House officials have struggled to expand access to mortgages, which have been hard for some borrowers to get since the financial crisis despite a sharp rise in home prices. But the likely moves by Fannie and Freddie could provide kindling for critics who worry about repeating some of the mistakes that led to the housing boom and bust.

The agreements wouldn t end the debate over the future of Fannie and Freddie, which were taken over by the government near the height of the crisis in 2008. Earlier this year, a bipartisan Senate plan to replace Fannie and Freddie as part of a broader housing-finance overhaul stalled.

The latest moves underscore just how much the government is relying on Fannie and Freddie to help increase the supply of credit. Steps to reduce down-payment standards would further signal a turn away from previous efforts to shrink the influence of the two mortgage giants.

The potential agreement  would allow credit to flow more freely to lower- and middle-income households,  said Mark Zandi, chief economist at Moody sAnalytics.  That s vital to getting the housing recovery moving forward. 

But Mark Calabria, director of financial-regulation studies at the libertarian Cato Institute, said:  This is the sort of thing that gets people underwater. Three percent [down payments] can disappear and become zero real quick. 

While Fannie and Freddie don t make loans, they buy them from lenders, package the loans into securities and then give guarantees to make investors whole if borrowers don t repay.

Fannie and Freddie have recouped tens of billions of dollars in penalties from lenders in recent years over claims that the lenders made underwriting mistakes on loans they sold to the mortgage giants during the run-up to the housing crash. Lenders have blamed those penalties, in which lenders were required to buy back mortgages, for prompting lenders to make loans only to borrowers with near-pristine credit.

In previous years, Fannie s and Freddie s regulator had tried to shrink their outsize role in the mortgage market. However, under new director Mel Watt, who took office in January, the FHFA has turned to expanding mortgage access, partly to ensure that tight credit doesn t stifle the housing recovery.

The FHFA has made several previous attempts to ease lenders  concerns. Beginning in 2013, the agency said lenders wouldn t have to buy back most loans where the borrower didn t miss any payments for three years. In May, the FHFA said borrowers could miss two nonconsecutive payments within three years.

In both cases, the FHFA exempted some mistakes such as fraud. But without clear definitions of what mistakes met that threshold, lenders said they remained wary of expanding mortgage access.

The new agreement would clarify which mistakes should constitute fraud, giving greater confidence to lenders that they won t be penalized many years after a loan is made. Still, lenders and regulators must reach an accord on what to do if they disagree on some problems, which could involve using a third-party arbiter to resolve disputes.

Mr. Watt is scheduled to speak at a Mortgage Bankers Association conference on Monday.

 Assuming Director Watt announces some new framework to give better clarity for lenders to extend credit to more qualified families, this will be a good step forward for the housing market,  David Stevens, the trade group s president, said in a statement.

Analysts at Compass Point Research & Trading LLC said an agreement would reinforce  our belief that policy makers are willing to embrace initiatives aimed at increasing the flow of mortgage credit. 

Separately, Fannie Mae, Freddie Mac and the FHFA are considering new programs that would allow the companies to guarantee some mortgages with down payments of as little as 3%. The program might be limited to certain kinds of loans, such as mortgages to first-time home buyers.

Fannie Mae stopped accepting loans with 3% down payments last year, except in certain circumstances, while Freddie stopped guaranteeing such mortgages years ago.

The companies already guarantee loans with down payments of as little as 5%, though loans with down payments of less than 20% typically require mortgage insurance.

Regulators and lenders have tried to walk a fine line between expanding mortgage access and moving too far toward the loose credit that led to the crisis.

In May, Mr. Watt said Fannie and Freddie should focus on making credit more available, rather than pull back from the mortgage market.

The potential impact of looser lending standards and low down-payment programs on the availability of mortgages isn t clear. One question: Will an agreement be enough to get lenders to boost their lending to lower-quality borrowers?

Meanwhile, the U.S. government and shareholders of Fannie and Freddie are continuing to fight in the courts over how to handle the companies  profits.

When the two companies were taken over in 2008 and put into a so-called conservatorship, the government didn t wipe out shareholders. Instead, it received warrants to buy as much as 80% of the common stock in Fannie and Freddie and a new class of preferred shares that initially paid a 10% dividend.

In August 2012, the government did away with the fixed dividend but required Fannie and Freddie to steer almost all of their profits to the U.S. Treasury.

Investors sued the government over the change in terms. A judge recently dismissed one of the most prominent cases, but others haven t been decided yet.

MANSION --- Jumbo Jungle: Score One for the Borrower --- Bullish mortgage lenders are lowering the minimum FICO scores borrowers need to qualify for a jumbo loan

By Anya Martin 
782 words
17 October 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
M13
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

As in any game, it helps to know the score.

Today a borrower may be able to get a jumbo loan with a FICO score as low as 680 out of a possible 850, though many lenders say they draw the line at anything below 720. The best terms go to borrowers with scores of 760 and above, says Keith Gumbinger, vice president of HSH.com, which tracks mortgage rates.

In 2006 -- before the mortgage crisis -- jumbo borrowers could qualify with scores in the 600s. After the real-estate bubble, however, jumbo mortgages dried up. Typically, only home buyers with a FICO score of 780 or above qualified for one.

Now, lenders are feeling bullish about getting more jumbos on their books, which can be more profitable than selling the loans to investors as mortgage-backed securities, says Mr. Gumbinger. When loans are kept in a lender's portfolio, the lender can set its own borrower qualifications, he adds.

Jumbo mortgages exceed Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac conventional-loan limits of $417,000 in most places and $625,500 in some high-price areas.

Milford, Conn.-based Total Mortgage has been able to introduce one 30-year fixed jumbo product with a 680 minimum score because of one private-equity-fund investor who is comfortable with that risk level, says John Walsh, CEO of Total Mortgage. The loan amount goes up to $1.5 million, requires a 20% down payment and has an interest rate about 0.25 points higher than the rate a borrower with a 740 score would receive, Mr. Walsh says.

Jacksonville, Fla.-based EverBank issues few jumbo mortgages to borrowers with scores below 720, preferring scores in the 760 range, says Tom Wind, executive vice president of home lending. "Below that score, we'd be looking for significant compensating factors, such as a lower loan-to-value ratio (LTV) and maybe more cash reserves," he adds. The LTV weighs the loan amount against the value of the property being purchased.

EverBank holds the majority of its jumbos in its portfolio, so it can be more flexible with qualification terms, Mr. Wind says. For example, an otherwise creditworthy borrower may have damaged credit because of an extenuating circumstance, such as a divorce, he explains.

Some smaller lenders, including many credit unions, say they don't require minimum credit scores but look at the full credit profile of the borrower.

Still, even lenders with some leeway built into their underwriting models take a hard look at credit scores, says Bob Walters, chief economist for Quicken Loans. "These minimums aren't suggestions, they are hard lines," he adds. "If you don't have the score, you don't get the loan." Quicken's minimum credit score to qualify for a jumbo mortgage is 680.

Fair Isaac Corp., a publicly traded company, developed FICO, the most commonly used method for determining credit scores. FICO is used by TransUnion, Equifax and Experian, the nation's three biggest credit agencies. Its analytics software assesses the risk that a borrower will become 90 days or more past due in monthly loan payments.

Beyond using minimum scores for qualifications, lenders may sort borrowers into tiers to determine the interest rate of the mortgage. Tiers generally encompass 20-point ranges, such as 700-719, 720-739, 740-759, etc., Mr. Walters says. Another competing method of evaluating risk, called VantageScore, is used by some lenders and mortgage-backed securities.

Here are a few more considerations for jumbo borrowers applying for a loan:

-- Married while borrowing separately. In the case of a home purchase or refinanced loan with two borrowers, the lender will assess risk using the lower credit score. Lower scorers could be removed from the application if one person's score is significantly lower and the other person's higher score meets income qualifications.

-- Raise the score. Especially if the borrower has a score slightly below the next lending tier, it might be worth the time to delay a home purchase and take steps raise to the score.

-- Now and later. A borrower who can't delay a home purchase could qualify with a lower score and refinance later if the score improves. Regardless, a quarter-point difference between a 4.25% loan for a 740 FICO score and a 4.5% loan for a 680 FICO score on a $1 million loan is only about $146 a month -- not a significant amount for most jumbo borrowers, Mr. Walsh says.

Banks Make It Easier to Score a Home Loan; Bullish mortgage lenders are lowering the minimum FICO scores borrowers need to get a jumbo loan.

Tech
KKR to Invest in Artificial Intelligence  Software Firm Arago; Private-Equity Firm Will Pay About $55 Million for a Minority Stake in German Startup

By Simon Clark 
420 words
16 October 2014
03:04
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

KKR & Co. is investing about $55 million in arago AG, a German firm that develops artificial intelligence software to replace information-technology workers.

The New York-based private-equity firm will buy a minority stake and plans to help develop the profitable startup into a global company. Arago s founder said his technology enables companies to cut IT workers and to reassign effective staff to more creative tasks.

 There will be jobs lost,  Hans-Christian Boos, arago s founder, said in an interview.  On the other hand, we see this massive demand for good IT people, and they re not around because they are busy. 

Global spending on IT services and software will grow about 4.9% to $1.35 trillion in 2015, according to research firm Gartner Inc. Arago competes with companies such as IPsoft.

To start globalizing arago s customer base, KKR will introduce the technology to managers among the more than 90 companies in which it owns stakes. Those companies spend a combined $10 billion a year on IT, according to KKR.

 Our goal is really to help him [Mr. Boos] to bring this to the U.S.,  Lucian Schnefelder, a director at KKR, said in an interview.  The problem you very often have with European, and in particular German companies, is they have a very, very strong underlying technology but they are not very sophisticated in selling and marketing it. 

Mr. Boos said arago s technology is the result of his 20-year quest to teach a machine human problem-solving behavior.  I think we are definitely the only ones that have brought it out as a commercial product,  he said.

After developing the problem-solving technology, arago s team decided to apply it to IT because  there are lots of problems in IT,  he said.

The technology could disrupt the outsourcing of IT tasks from Europe and North America to low-wage countries such as India.

 In India you have some people who have excellent educations and we make them waste their time by clicking the same three buttons every day. There will certainly be tasks lost for people who just stick to doing processes that machines can do better,  Mr. Boos said.  If you show us your staff costs we will say that every ticket that we do is half what your people will do.

Global Finance: Stock Dive Hits Hedge Funds

By Rob Copeland 
679 words
15 October 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

The recent turmoil in financial markets is upending some of the biggest hedge-fund trades of the year.

A plunge in energy prices this month and a recent legal setback for managers making bets on shares of mortgage companies Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, as well as swings in technology shares, have wiped billions of dollars of market value from the stock market and wounded many hedge funds.

Losses this month compound the difficulties facing the industry after a quarter in which stock-focused hedge funds on average lost money even as broad stock indexes rose, according to fund tracker HFR Inc.

The damage is being amplified by many hedge funds' tendency to buy and sell the same stocks, analysts and traders said, a practice known as herding that can boost returns when markets are rising but lead to sharp drops when sentiment reverses.

"One person gets out, another sees the price move and then you've got a stampede," said Faryan Amir-Ghassemi of Novus Partners Inc., an analytics firm for institutional investors.

A closely followed list of the stocks most prized by hedge-fund managers has tumbled 8.1% since Sept. 1, compared with a 4.8% fall for the S&P 500, according to Goldman Sachs Group.

Claren Road Asset Management LLC, a unit of Carlyle Group LP with $8 billion under management, is down more than 10% for October, according to people familiar with its results, on track for the worst month since it was formed 19 years ago.

The firm has been bruised by pullbacks in energy firms such as natural-gas providers Cheniere Energy Inc. and Liquefied Natural Gas Ltd., as energy prices have fallen amid concerns about the pace of global growth. Cheniere is down 21% this month, after shares rose 326% from the end of 2012 to this past September.

Also hitting Claren Road: a sharp decline in Fannie and Freddie shares following an adverse ruling in an investor lawsuit. Claren Road, which owns shares in the mortgage-finance companies, called the ruling a setback in a message to clients.

Owl Creek Asset Management LP is down 5% in October and has doubled its losses for the year, following a tumble in technology stocks such as T-Mobile US Inc., people familiar with the fund said. Bets on firms such as Micron Technology Inc. have weighed on Coatue Management LLC, down 7% year to date, according to people familiar with the firm.

The hedge-fund exodus is causing some unlikely market moves. Airline stocks typically rally when oil prices fall, cutting their fuel costs. But shares of American Airlines Group Inc. have fallen 12% in October, while Delta Air Lines Inc. and United Continental Holdings Inc. dropped about 9% and 7%, respectively, in part, because of the heavy concentration of hedge-fund holders of the stocks, traders said. The steep decline mirrors an even sharper rise in the stocks earlier in the year as hedge funds and others piled into the industry.

One trader said a number of stock-focused funds went into negative territory for the year at the end of last week, prompting some to begin selling positions and turn defensive.

Hedge funds justify some of the highest fees on Wall Street by promising to make money even in volatile conditions. But as the industry has expanded -- nearing $3 trillion, a threefold rise over a decade -- managers have tended to cluster into the same trades. Many hedge-fund managers had lagged behind market indexes this year before the recent tumult, creating an incentive to buy or sell volatile stocks in the hope of catching a quick rise.

Tide Point Capital Management LP, the two-year-old hedge fund backed by industry veteran Christopher Shumway, surrendered one-third of its 2014 winnings in the first two weeks of October, dropping 8%, said a person familiar with the fund.

---

Christian Berthelsen, Juliet Chung, Matt Wirz and Matt Jarzemsky contributed to this article.

Business Briefs

486 words
14 October 2014
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
22
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

MEDIA

GTCR in Talks for Gorkana

Private-equity firm GTCR is in exclusive talks to buy media-monitoring company Gorkana Group, according to people familiar with the matter.

Gorkana, a U.K.-based firm that, among other things, maintains a database of journalists for use by public-relations professionals, could fetch more than GBP 150 million ($240 million), the people said.

Exponent Private Equity, which bought Gorkana in 2010, merged the business with Durrants Media Monitoring. Durrants previously acquired media analysis company Metrica Inc.

Chicago-based GTCR earlier this year acquired two public relations software companies, Sweden's Cision AB and Vocus Inc. Last month, Cision and Vocus said they would buy Visible Technologies, a social media engagement and analytics company.

-- Gillian Tan

---

MUTUAL FUNDS

Fidelity Appoints New CEO

Fidelity Investments has named Abigail "Abby" Johnson chief executive, according to an internal memo sent by the mutual-fund firm Monday and viewed by The Wall Street Journal.

Ms. Johnson, 52, will take over the position from her father, Edward "Ned" Johnson III, who is 84 years old and has been CEO since 1977. Mr. Johnson, whose father founded the company in 1946, will remain chairman, according to the memo.

Ms. Johnson holds the position of president of FMR LLC, the parent company of Fidelity Investments, and she will keep that role. The firm, with about $2 trillion of assets under management, is the second-largest mutual-fund firm in the country, after Vanguard Group.

"Along with our senior team, we will work together to strengthen Fidelity's industry leadership and to innovate in ways that enhance the experience we provide to our customers," Mr. Johnson wrote.

-- Kirsten Grind

---

ADVERTISING

Publicis Buys Matomy Stake

Publicis Groupe SA is buying a 20% stake in digital-advertising technology company Matomy Media Group Ltd., in the latest move by a large ad agency to add technology capabilities as more marketing dollars shift online.

The acquisition is part of the French group's strategy to expand, in part by making small acquisitions, after it abandoned a planned merger with U.S. rival Omnicom Group Inc.

Matomy, based in Tel Aviv but listed in London earlier this year, said Publicis would acquire 17.9 million of its shares, or 20% of the company, for 227 pence ($3.65) a share. Publicis also has an option to buy a further 4.4 million shares at the same price, meaning the deal could be valued at as much as GBP 51 million ($82 million) for a 24.9% stake.

The digital-ad tech company said the partnership would give it the benefit of the French ad giant's media-buying and digital-advertising capabilities. Matomy operates a technology platform assessing the performance of online advertising and charges advertisers based on the performance of their ads rather than upfront fees.

-- Rory Gallivan and Inti Landauro

Business
GTCR in Talks to Buy Gorkana Group; U.K.-based Media Monitoring Firm Could Fetch More Than $240 Million

By Gillian Tan 
196 words
13 October 2014
16:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Private-equity firm GTCR is in exclusive talks to buy media-monitoring company Gorkana Group, according to people familiar with the matter.

Gorkana, a U.K.-based firm that, among other things, maintains a database of journalists for use by public-relations professionals, could fetch more than 150 million pounds ($240 million), the people said.

Phoenix Equity Partners Ltd. in June sold a majority stake in U.K.-based Precise Media Group to WPP PLC s research arm Kantar, while Australia s Quadrant Private Equity in June sold a majority stake in media-intelligence company iSentia Ltd. through an initial public offering.

Business Briefs

555 words

MEDIA

GTCR in Talks for Gorkana

Private-equity firm GTCR is in exclusive talks to buy media-monitoring company Gorkana Group, according to people familiar with the matter.

Gorkana, a U.K.-based firm that, among other things, maintains a database of journalists for use by public-relations professionals, could fetch more than 150 million ($240 million), the people said.

Phoenix Equity Partners Ltd. in June sold a majority stake in U.K.-based Precise Media Group to WPP PLC s research arm Kantar, while Australia s Quadrant Private Equity in June sold a majority stake in media-intelligence company iSentia Ltd. through an initial public offering.

Gillian Tan

MUTUAL FUNDS

Fidelity Appoints New CEO

Fidelity Investments has named Abigail  Abby  Johnson chief executive, according to an internal memo sent by the mutual-fund firm Monday and viewed by The Wall Street Journal.

Ms. Johnson, 52, will take over the position from her father, Edward  Ned  Johnson III, who is 84 years old and has been CEO since 1977. Mr. Johnson, whose father founded the company in 1946, will remain chairman, according to the memo.

Ms. Johnson holds the position of president of FMR LLC, the parent company of Fidelity Investments, and she will keep that role. The firm, with about $2 trillion of assets under management, is the second-largest mutual-fund firm in the country, after Vanguard Group.

 Along with our senior team, we will work together to strengthen Fidelity s industry leadership and to innovate in ways that enhance the experience we provide to our customers,  Mr. Johnson wrote.

Kirsten Grind

ADVERTISING

Publicis Groupe SA is buying a 20% stake in digital-advertising technology company Matomy Media Group Ltd., in the latest move by a large ad agency to add technology capabilities as more marketing dollars shift online.

The acquisition is part of the French group s strategy to expand, in part by making small acquisitions, after it abandoned a planned merger with U.S. rival Omnicom Group Inc.

Matomy, based in Tel Aviv but listed in London earlier this year, said Publicis would acquire 17.9 million of its shares, or 20% of the company, for 227 pence ($3.65) a share. Publicis also has an option to buy a further 4.4 million shares at the same price, meaning the deal could be valued at as much as 51 million ($82 million) for a 24.9% stake.

The digital-ad tech company said the partnership would give it the benefit of the French ad giant s media-buying and digital-advertising capabilities. Matomy operates a technology platform assessing the performance of online advertising and charges advertisers based on the performance of their ads rather than upfront fees.

Matomy Chief Executive Ofer Druker said the tie-up shows big ad companies  understand the need to be more active in  furthering their technology.

 Big data  challenge perplexes fund managers

By Madison Marriage 
1,117 words
12 October 2014
09:07
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Asking new employees to hand over urine samples for drug testing might seem distasteful to some, or alarming to others, but it has become relatively normal practice in corporate America.

This fact has not escaped the notice of one of the world's biggest private equity companies, which has a majority stake in a large manufacturer of drug screening tests in the US.

The private equity company found that analysing sales data from the drug-test manufacturer could help it build an early picture of whether US employment rates were going up or down: the more people being hired, the more drug screens sold. This information could in turn be used to guide its investments in public equity markets.

This is just one example of how asset managers are beginning to realise the potential "big data" - analysing large amounts of information from previously inaccessible or overlooked sources - has for their investment processes, according to Craig Hapelt, a partner at Boston Consulting Group.

He says: "Everyone is analysing data, that is a given. But analysing it on a very wide scale and bringing in different pieces of data that have not been in place before, that is new."

Although fund managers recognise that big data could provide valuable insights, many remain uncertain about how to apply vast data sets from sources as disparate as Twitter, the social networking service, Starbucks, the coffee chain, or Google Flu Trends, which analyses internet search terms to track global flu activity, to their investment processes.

Nick Thomas, a partner at Baillie Gifford, the Edinburgh-based fund house, says: "We are absolutely fascinated by the power of big data and how it makes Google a fantastic business, and we are some of the biggest holders of [companies] such as Amazon and Baidu, who are at the cutting edge of using data to build amazing internet businesses. But trying to fit some of those ideas into our investment process is difficult because we take a very long-term, slow approach to investing. We are thinking about it, but it is causing a lot of head scratching."

Schroders' head of investment, Peter Harrison, agrees that asset managers need to get to grips with big data in order to remain competitive, but a spokesperson for the company said it has "nothing set in stone" on this front.

Mr Harrison told FTfm in July: "Asset managers have got to get much smarter [and] work out how we can use the vast amount of data out there more effectively. Using data effectively will give you the vital, winning edge."

Mr Hapelt says he has worked with a large US pension fund and several sovereign wealth funds who are interested in how they can harness big data to improve their in-house investment processes. Many big investors have only started showing an interest in this area in the past 12 months.

The fact that fund groups have arguably been slow to realise the potential big data has to offer - both in terms of enhancing investment returns, and in understanding their own client base - has fuelled fears in the industry that internet companies with a better handle on data analytics, such as Facebook or Amazon, could easily enter asset management.

"I suspect that [Google's artificial intelligence] people could clone an asset management stalwart before breakfast," an FT reader recently wrote to this newspaper.

One branch of the asset management community ahead of the game is the macro hedge fund set that use a computer-driven investment process, according to Armando Gonzalez, chief executive of RavenPack, a data analytics company that works with more than half of the world's biggest quantitative hedge funds.

Mr Gonzalez says this section of the hedge fund industry has been purchasing his company's analytics, which attempt to draw out market-moving company information from social media and news feeds, for several years, but that there has been a "significant" uptick in business from mainstream asset managers in the last two years.

The obvious drawback of using big data sourced from areas such as social media to influence investment decisions is that public sentiment cannot detect events such as insider trading, or substantial changes to monetary policy.

But it can raise red flags on a company when others are perhaps too optimistic, Mr Gonzalez says. UK supermarket Tesco, for example, had a low score in RavenPack's metrics (just 20 out of 100) over the summer. The score is based on factors such as shifts in analyst and social media sentiment towards a company.

This insight might have proved useful to investors such as Warren Buffett, who made the unusual move of stating he regretted his investment in Tesco after it emerged the retailer had overstated its forecast profits by 250m.

Deciding whether to change positions when big data flags up interesting patterns is the next challenge for fund groups, according to Michael Hintze, chief executive of CQS, the $14bn multi-strategy asset management company.

He says: "One of the biggest challenges [in asset management] is how you filter and channel the 'noise' or 'unstructured data' and harness that. You also need the context and the ability to time the execution of the trade [based on that data] appropriately."

Professor David Hand, chief scientific adviser at UK hedge fund group Winton, agrees: "There is more and more data captured every second. But data alone does not give you the answers. You have to ask the right questions [as] we are looking for very weak signals in a lot of noise. Only then can you attempt to predict how markets might behave."

Winton's research department has 140 people who specialise in data analysis and the company is "constantly hiring new people" in this area, according to Prof Hand.

Making such a large commitment to assessing big data trends is a scary concept for more traditional asset managers, according to Mr Hapelt.

"Some [fund groups] say [their ability to assess data] is such a mess, they can't aspire to be any good. The hard part is [deciding whether to] invest in a whole load of stuff that might deliver in five years' time, or might not get there. That is the fear."

The consultant estimates that roughly 15 per cent of the asset management companies he works with have begun to organise their approach to big data to improve investment performance, and many are hiring specifically in this area.

To get on top of the opportunities big data can provide, Mr Hapelt believes fund groups need to establish internally who is responsible for data management. Appointing a "chief data officer" could be the first step.

Heard on the Street / Financial Analysis and Commentary

1,234 words
10 October 2014
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
32
English
Copyright  2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Tianhe Requires

Convincing Catalyst

In the latest battle between short sellers and Chinese firms, the shorts so far have the upper hand.

When the secretive group Anonymous Analytics lobbed a fraud-allegation bomb on to Tianhe Chemicals a month ago, the Hong Kong-listed company halted trading in its shares and spent time building a defense. Yet Tianhe's 55-page rebuttal was lacking. When shares resumed trading Thursday, its shares plunged 40% and are now 23% below its initial-offering price from June.

Anonymous argues that the chemicals firm overstates sales and profits. The group, which has no identified members, produced local-government filings of Tianhe's two operating subsidiaries that called into question reported profit in the company's IPO prospectus. Tianhe has shot back saying that Anonymous's documents are the fabricated ones, and contends that the subsidiaries' numbers aren't at odds.

The key here is the subsidiaries' accounts and tax receipts. While Tianhe got letters from tax authorities after the allegations arose that confirm payments, it is disappointing that the company isn't making the original tax filings widely available. It says it will only show a copy to those who come to its office over the next two weeks with a physical share certificate, because the information is confidential and amounts to unwieldy binders of documents. This oddly excludes new potential investors.

Another bone of contention is Tianhe's sales of a special chemical called antimar that repels fingerprints from surfaces such as smartphones. Anonymous says the amount of antimar Tianhe purports to sell is too much given the global smartphone market, to which Tianhe responds that antimar is used in multiple industries, even casino slot machines, says Joseph Lee, the chief financial officer. Tianhe says it sells antimar to trading houses, and doesn't know the final customers. Investors looking for Tianhe to destroy these allegations might have expected more detail on who exactly uses its products.

That Tianhe didn't furnish more evidence is one reason investors exited the stock Wednesday. Equally, though, these arguments are inconvenient when the stock was highly valued. Tianhe's enterprise value is now 8.6 times trailing earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization, in line with peer Japan's Daikin Industries, but well below the 15.3 times it traded before the allegations. Investors are no longer willing to give the company credit for higher margins and faster growth.

Tianhe's shares may rise a bit since executives say they may buy them for their personal accounts. Yet a truly restorative rally will only come if the company makes more convincing arguments.

-- Abheek Bhattacharya

Fed Fear Imperils ECB Strategy

Somewhere at the European Central Bank, a warning light is flashing: The euro has bounced.

That is a problem. The ECB is counting on a weaker euro to boost inflation from its current nadir of 0.3%. True, the exchange-rate reversal is small: The euro has risen to $1.27 from just above $1.25 last week. But this is a departure from the near one-way movement in the single currency that started in May.

The ECB faces a battle on several fronts. The first is the Federal Reserve's apparent unwillingness to think about raising interest rates. Markets may have applauded the dovish tone of Wednesday's Fed minutes, but they won't have gone down well at the ECB. The Fed's worries about the dollar seem overdone; on a historical trade-weighted basis, the dollar remains weak. The U.S. central bank's concerns about a stalled European recovery are more valid, however.

But the ECB is partly relying on divergence in monetary policies between the U.S. and the eurozone to deliver a weaker euro. If the Fed puts off tightening, that force is diminished -- particularly as markets are skeptical of the ECB's efforts to boost its balance sheet. The risk is that if the Fed's worries about the dollar and Europe cause it to delay rate increases, it will generate more problems for Europe -- and more worries for the Fed.

Second, even as the euro has fallen, so has the price of oil. Brent crude has dropped to $91 a barrel from $115 in June. The slide in the euro has cushioned that: UniCredit recently calculated that if the euro were still at $1.40, the slide in oil prices would have pushed eurozone inflation to zero. A lower oil price should, of course, be a boon to consumers and businesses, reducing costs. But for eurozone inflation, further declines are bad news.

Of course, this may just be a correction for the euro, and the single currency may resume its fall. But the underlying problem for the ECB is it has to rely on other independent actors playing their part. In an ideal world for the ECB, the Fed would tighten policy, eurozone governments would undertake structural overhauls to boost growth, and the drag on inflation from energy and food prices would fade. For now, the world is far from ideal.

-- Richard Barley

Oil Markets Fly Into a Perfect Storm

Oil bulls aghast at the recent slump in prices should avoid looking at airline stocks.

Airline investors usually love falling fuel prices. But in the past month, airline stocks have joined oil in its slide that extends back to late June.

Airlines have actually been doing well after a round of restructuring and consolidation. But their stocks have succumbed to wider fears about slowing economic growth. The International Monetary Fund just downgraded its global forecasts again.

Besides the eurozone, the IMF also cut projections across large parts of the developing world -- which accounts for the vast bulk of growth in demand. While the IMF didn't cut forecasts for China, oil demand there is growing slowly. As of August, demand in the year to date was up just 3.1%, according to Deutsche Bank estimates, setting up what could be the weakest year for growth in oil consumption there since 2005.

All this complicates things for oil bulls praying supply cuts will support prices. Hopes rest on Saudi Arabia unilaterally taking barrels off the market. But so far it has chosen to defend its market share and seems to be banking on lower oil prices forcing some higher-cost rivals offline instead.

One target: the U.S. exploration-and-production industry's rising output. But E&P firms don't necessarily turn the taps off immediately when prices fall; look at Wednesday's release of data showing a big build in U.S. oil inventories.

Hedges and productivity gains provide some protection for those companies and once capital has been sunk into land and infrastructure, they often try to ride out weaker prices before capitulating.

Energy economist Phil Verleger points out that in previous instances in which Saudi Arabia let prices fall, in the mid-1980s and late 1990s, oil dropped drastically before other producers fell into line. Right now, a weakening demand picture puts even more of an onus onto producing countries, ranging from Russia to Nigeria, to rein in output.

Eventually, the pain of falling prices will get too much for those producers to bear. Meanwhile, getting there likely will involve a lot more pain for anyone invested in oil.

-- Liam Denning

Tianhe Stock Sinks After Halt Is Lifted

By Jacky Wong and Yvonne Lee 
657 words
10 October 2014
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
25
English
Copyright  2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

HONG KONG -- Shares of Tianhe Chemicals Group, the Chinese company accused by "hacktivist" group Anonymous Analytics of falsifying some of its financial reporting, fell 40% after a monthlong trading halt was lifted.

Late Wednesday, the company released a 55-page rebuttal to Anonymous Analytics' accusations. It said allegations by the group amounted to "twisted facts and intertwined fiction."

In early September, Anonymous Analytics released a report alleging that Tianhe, which had been a market darling after listing its stock in Hong Kong, falsified its accounting, revenue and margins and overstated sales of a lucrative chemical agent called anti-mar. Shares in Tianhe had been suspended since Sept. 2 and began trading Thursday.

Anonymous Analytics said on its website Thursday that the Chinese company hadn't responded to its satisfaction. It said it is sticking to its initial claims and issued a report countering the company's statement.

In a media briefing Thursday, Tianhe Chief Executive Wei Xuan said that he has started to buy Tianhe shares from the market for his personal account to restore investor confidence in the company. He also said that the company is looking into taking legal action against Anonymous Analytics, and will decide on whether to do so in the next few days.

"We were shocked by Anonymous Analytics' allegations, which are groundless. We believe these allegations have been made to undermine Tianhe's reputation and to manipulate the share price," Mr. Wei said.

Anonymous Analytics couldn't immediately be reached for comment on Mr. Wei's latest remarks.

Tianhe, which is partly owned by Morgan Stanley, fell as much as 47% Thursday morning to an intraday low of HK$1.22, which is 32% lower than the price it sold at in a Hong Kong initial public offering in June. The stock closed down 40% at HK$1.39.

The company raised US$748 million via the Hong Kong IPO. Between the offering and the initial report from Anonymous Analytics, Tianhe shares rose 28%, outperforming the benchmark Hang Seng Index, which rose 6.7% over the same period.

Mr. Wei said the Anonymous Analytics allegations haven't affected the company's operations. Tianhe's third-quarter gross profit rose 44% from a year earlier to 1.22 billion Chinese yuan (US$198.6 million).

Anonymous claimed last month that reported revenue for two Tianhe units, Jinzhou DPF-TH and Fuxin Hengtong, was much lower than the company said, citing accounts that the company submitted to China's State Administration of Industry and Commerce.

Anonymous Analytics, which calls itself an advocate for corporate transparency but doesn't disclose who its members are, said in its report Thursday that it wouldn't profit from its allegations.

A statement noted, though, that while it holds "no direct or indirect interest" in Tianhe, "you may assume that certain of Anonymous Analytics' research and due diligence contacts, consultants, affiliates and/or clients may have a short position in the stock."

On Wednesday, Tianhe said its shareholders can inspect the financial statements and tax receipts of the two units if they can prove they are registered stockholders.

"This type of anonymous and hyperbolic market discourse has no accountability for truth," the company said in its filing to Hong Kong's stock exchange. "The company, on the other hand, is fully responsible for every fact that we meticulously set forth in the pages to follow."

Tianhe also denied Anonymous' allegations that its key customers had ties to the company and that it overstated anti-mar sales. Anti-mar is a chemical agent used in products including smartphones and cameras.

"We have worked very hard in the past twenty years to come to this stage, but what Anonymous Analytics had done is spending little money to make false allegation to attack us and our shareholders," Mr. Wei said.

Calpers Aims to Boost Real-Estate Investment

By Craig Karmin and Dan Fitzpatrick 
826 words
9 October 2014
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
25
English
Copyright  2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

SACRAMENTO -- As it pulls the plug on hedge funds, the country's biggest public pension is giving its property portfolio a $7 billion jolt.

The California Public Employees' Retirement System plans to increase its $26 billion of commercial-real-estate investments by 27%, according to its latest asset-allocation report. The boost would leave Calpers with its largest property holdings since before the financial crisis.

More than a decade ago, Calpers started loading up on funds investing in more speculative properties like shopping malls and office developments in a bid to boost returns. Those bets proved ill-timed, as the recession and commercial-property bust led to losses of about $10 billion, or about half the value of its portfolio during the downturn.

The latest move is different, Calpers says. This time it is focusing on investments such as fully leased office towers and apartments in big cities, which it argues are safer because there is established demand for these properties. In another shift, the giant pension fund has been investing almost exclusively through real-estate funds that manage separate accounts created for Calpers, which offers more control over how that money is invested.

Calpers' real-estate play is emblematic of a larger movement by big investors in recent years toward safer assets such as Treasury bonds.

Prices of higher-quality properties have shot up 82% over the past five years and now are above their precrisis high in 2007, according to the Green Street Advisors Commercial Property Price Index. The capitalization rate for major metropolitan office buildings has fallen to about 6% from 8% in 2010, according to real-estate researcher Real Capital Analytics. The cap rate, a valuation measure, is a property's annual income divided by the purchase price.

Some of Calpers' real-estate consultants are warning that moving too much money into pricey properties could backfire. Pension Consulting Alliance Inc. cautioned in a July report to Calpers not to expect "these increases in value to be sustained when interest rates and new construction starts return to more normalized levels."

Ted Eliopoulos, Calpers' recently appointed chief investment officer, changed the fund's real-estate approach in 2011, when he led that group. Since then, the fund has delivered average annual returns of 14% in its real-estate portfolio. But Mr. Eliopoulos acknowledges the recent high returns are unsustainable.

The fund's goals now are to diversify its portfolio risk and generate steady, modest gains, rather than striving for outsize returns with more speculative bets, he said. Likewise, Calpers on Sept. 15 said it would shed its $4 billion investment in hedge funds as part of an effort to simplify its assets and reduce costs.

"Our strategy is to focus on high-quality real estate," Mr. Eliopoulos said. "We're still on track."

Other big investors are taking a different course, eschewing pricey blue-chip properties in favor of half-rented office buildings, shopping malls in transition and other riskier ventures with lower price tags. A new survey this month from real-estate consultant Hodes Weill & Associates and Cornell University of 231 large investors found that three-quarters said they were moving money into these kinds of properties in search of higher returns.

After the dot-com bust of 2000, Calpers turned to real estate for the double-digit gains it no longer could count on from stocks. That meant putting money into higher-risk real-estate funds that used up to 80% levels of debt while aiming for returns of 20% or more.

With prices rising, the riskier, debt-oriented strategy worked at first. For the fiscal year ended June 2006, the real-estate portfolio returned 35%. But the losses began to mount during the financial crisis.

A symbol of Calpers' crisis-era stumbles can be found in an empty tract just three blocks from its Sacramento headquarters.

Before the 2007-2008 bust, Calpers was part of a plan to build twin 53-story condominium towers on a block-long parcel that leads to the state capitol building. But financing for the condos fell through in 2007, and the land now is pockmarked with trees, brown grass and pilings driven into the ground.

Calpers, which contributed $100 million to the plan, hopes the proximity to an established business district and other new projects ultimately will attract new development.

Calpers has raised its target allocation for real estate to 11%, which would require investing another $7 billion in property, based on the fund's nearly $300 billion in total assets.

Mr. Eliopoulos said that as a long-term investor, he is less worried about possible price fluctuations. He added that while Calpers' new real-estate target calls for investing billions of additional dollars in real estate, the guidelines allow him to be a little above or below the target level for flexibility.

Thursday 04.00 BST. Japanese, Australian and Hong Kong stocks all rebounded from sharp losses the previous day, the US central bank injected some positive sentiment into global markets.

Sydney's S&P/ASX 200 led the rebound, rising 1.4 per cent in spite of a muddled picture of the labour market resulting from significant revisions.

Australia's September employment report said 29,700 jobs were lost in the month, compared with predictions that 16,000 jobs would be created. The jobless rate rose from 6 per cent to 6.1 per cent.

However, confusion reigned in the aftermath of the report, causing the Australian dollar to fall 0.4 per cent before reclaiming a two-week high of $0.885.

The Australia Bureau of Statistics had flagged that the report would include months of revisions to strip out seasonal adjustment estimates that had proved to be unhelpful.

The 121,000 jobs supposedly created in August - a number considered not credible by many observers - was revised down to a gain of 32,100.

But a number of analysts said the report and its revisions failed to clarify the direction of the nation's jobs market.

"We are left in the dark as to the state of the labour market," said Annette Beacher, strategist at TD Securities. "This is not ideal given the RBA, Treasury and policy makers rely on such information about that health of the labour market to shape appropriate fiscal, monetary and social policy."

Elsewhere, Hong Kong's Hang Seng Index jumped 1.2 per cent, while Tokyo's Nikkei 225 rose 0.5 per cent and the Shanghai Composite added 0.3 per cent.

South Korean markets were closed to observe Alphabet Day.

Japanese stocks were bolstered by better than anticipated machinery orders data, which serve as a proxy for private capital expenditures. September orders were up 4.7 per cent month-on-month - a third straight monthly gain - versus predictions of a 0.5 per cent gain.

However, three months of increases pale in comparison to orders falling by a fifth in May - a reaction to Japan raising its national sales tax in April.

"Although machinery orders continued to recover in August, their current level still points to falling business investment in the third quarter, as does the decline in capital goods shipments," wrote Marcel Thieliant, economist at Capital Economics. "But the recovery in capital expenditure should resume soon."

The broad upward moves in Asian equity markets follow a bullish session in New York, where the S&P 500 finished 1.8 per cent higher - its biggest one-day rise since October 2013.

Sentiment was lifted thanks to the release of minutes of the Federal Reserve's past monetary policy meeting, which appeared particularly dovish to many analysts.

Fed board members expressed concerns that the US economy could be negatively impacted "if foreign economic growth came in weaker than anticipated".

"Right now, there is no incentive for the Fed to signal tighter policy," said Mazen Issa, strategist at TD Securities.

"Inflation is curling lower and a strong dollar coupled with sagging global growth implies that lower inflation remains a risk in the US economy," he added.

"Until there is evidence of a sustained improvement not only domestically but abroad, the Fed will take its time in addressing the outlook for monetary policy.

In corporate news, National Australia Bank warned that its full-year cash profits would fall 14 per cent year-on-year as it booked writedowns amounting to nearly A$1bn related to its troubled UK venture. It announced plans to raise more capital. Shares rose 1.3 per cent.

Meanwhile, shares of Tianhe Chemicals tumbled 37 per cent after a five-week trading suspension following allegations of accounting fraud.

The Chinese chemicals group released a 55-page rebuttal of fraud claims late on Wednesday night, arguing that Anonymous Analytics - a group claiming links to Anonymous, the shadowy hacker collective - "used fabricated financial statements to mislead investors".

Law firms find ways to speed up normally lengthy legal processes

By Jane Croft 
1,089 words
9 October 2014
00:05
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

For the past year a dark cloud of litigation risk has hung over the European and US banking sector, dragging down share prices.

Analysts at JPMorgan Cazenove recently estimated legal costs would set back UK banks a further 15bn over the next three years, on top of the 30bn they have set aside since 2011.

Dozens of banking institutions in Europe and the US, including Bank of America, HSBC, Lloyds, UBS and Deutsche Bank, have been facing an array of civil litigation and regulatory issues relating to their past, ranging from mis-selling of mortgage-backed securities and alleged sanctions-busting to conduct related to Libor and interest rate swaps.

The legal strategies that some banks are deploying to deal with their litigation and handle their regulatory risks are, therefore, being closely watched by others in the industry.

One example is how some of the Swiss banks handled the US Department of Justice's clampdown on offshore bank accounts as it sought to pursue institutions for allegedly helping US account holders commit tax evasion. Following an agreement with the Swiss government, the DoJ announced a programme in August 2013 through which it encouraged Swiss banks to co-operate with its investigations rather than face criminal proceedings. The programme, which meant institutions could reach a non-prosecution agreement with the DoJ if they met stringent requirements, was unique.

Since then, more than 100 institutions have been assessing whether to sign up to the programme. It has tough requirements, including paying penalties for all non-disclosed US accounts held by a bank since March 2009 and giving information on accounts held directly or indirectly by US taxpayers since August 2008.

The tabular content relating to this article is not available to view. Apologies in advance for the inconvenience caused.

About a third of the Swiss institutions affected turned to law firm Baker & McKenzie, which has offices in the US and Switzerland, for guidance. The firm's global approach and its Swiss partners helped bridge the cultural divide between the US and Switzerland - a crucial issue as the US pressed for total disclosure from a notoriously secretive industry. This included dealing with the thorny issue of data privacy - under Swiss law, employee data cannot be disclosed without the employee's consent. < >

Marnin Michaels, a tax partner with Baker & McKenzie in Zurich, says the programme was challenging for a number of reasons. "Typically, DoJ investigations take years, yet this was done within six months," he says. "At one time or another we worked with 40 or more institutions. We had to balance the local data protection laws with the US's need to have as much information as possible and we had to train our people to do this as quickly as possible to encourage clients into self-disclosure."

Other law firms have had to adopt an innovative approach to handling civil litigation brought by consumers who claimed they had been mis-sold payment protection insurance (PPI).

<>

In recent years, thousands of consumers in the UK have brought court proceedings, that have clogged up county courts in lengthy litigation. Where banks chose to settle, they faced paying the high legal costs of "no-win, no-fee" solicitors. An additional issue was that the PPI claims were all specific, so the banking industry could not bring a legal test case where a judgment would bind all subsequent claims - as the industry had done with consumer claims over current account charges in 2007.

However, law firm Simmons & Simmons, acting for Barclays, brought two legal cases that led to rulings clarifying the legal position. This effectively resulted in claims being directed away from the county courts into the bank's internal complaint functions.

In the first case of Andrew & Ors v Barclays, Mr Justice Waksman ruled in favour of the bank, staying PPI claims for eight weeks so that the bank could process them as complaints. He gave guidance that complaints should be brought first to the bank, rather than swamping the scarce resources of the court system.

A second ruling, Binns v Firstplus Financial, effectively meant any consumer who turned to the court after having their complaint upheld by the bank would see their claim struck out.

While the banking industry has been busy dealing with its legacy issues, the volume of litigation in the technology sector has increased significantly because laws have failed to keep up with the rapid pace of technological change.

One of the most significant rulings this year has been by the European Court of Justice, which gave European citizens the right to ask internet search engines to remove results for queries that included their name - the ruling was dubbed "the right to be forgotten".

Other cases have also trodden fresh ground in the developing area of intellectual property - law firm Garrigues won five cases for Google.

One notable case saw the Spanish court siding with Google and Garrigues on a dispute with broadcaster Telecinco, finding that Google's YouTube service did not have to check television clips for potential copyright violations before posting them online.

Other cases have involved trademark disputes between retailers, which are increasingly seeing the internet as a sales driver. Law firms have used new techniques, such as big data, to help them win these trademark cases. One example involved Marks and Spencer, which lost a long-running court battle after a judge ruled that the retailer had infringed the trademark of Interflora, the flower delivery business, by using it to promote M&S flowers on the internet.

Interflora had sued M&S in the High Court because it said the retailer had used its trademarked name as a keyword on Google, via the Google AdWords system. AdWords allows companies to buy online advertising that will appear when users type particular words into the search engine. As a result, typing in "Interflora" as a search term into Google brought up an M&S advert under the "sponsored links" heading, even though that advert bore no relationship to Interflora.

Law firm Pinsent Masons, which won the case for Interflora, broke new ground by using GoogleAnalytics and online metrics, rather than traditional survey evidence, to demonstrate to the court the confusion caused among consumers when they used Google.

This was the first time the courts had accepted this type of anonymised big data as evidence of consumer confusion - and demonstrates the value of using the latest tools.

Real Estate
Calpers Reboots Its Real-Estate Ambitions; Pension Fund to Boost Investments by $7 Billion

By Craig Karmin And Dan Fitzpatrick 
964 words
7 October 2014
20:08
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

SACRAMENTO As it pulls the plug on hedge funds, the country s biggest public pension is giving its property portfolio a $7 billion jolt.

The California Public Employees  Retirement System plans to increase its $26 billion of commercial real-estate investments by 27%, according to its latest asset-allocation report. The boost would leave Calpers with its largest property holdings since before the financial crisis.

More than a decade ago, Calpers started loading up on funds investing in more speculative properties like shopping malls and office developments in a bid to boost returns. Those bets proved ill-timed, as the recession and commercial-property bust led to losses of around $10 billion, or about half the value of its portfolio during the downturn.

The latest move is different, Calpers says. This time it is focusing on investments such as fully leased office towers and apartments in big cities, which it argues are safer because there is established demand for these properties. In another shift, the giant pension fund has been investing almost exclusively through real-estate funds that manage separate accounts created for Calpers, which offers more control over how that money is invested.

Calpers  real-estate play is emblematic of a larger movement by big investors in recent years toward safer assets such as Treasury bonds.

Prices of higher-quality properties have shot up 82% over the past five years and now are above their precrisis high in 2007, according to the Green Street Advisors Commercial Property Price Index. The capitalization rate for major metropolitan office buildings has fallen to about 6% from 8% in 2010, according to real-estate researcher Real Capital Analytics. The cap rate, a valuation measure, is a property s annual income divided by the purchase price.

Some of Calpers  real-estate consultants are warning that moving too much money into pricey properties could backfire. Pension Consulting Alliance Inc. cautioned in a July report to Calpers not to expect  these increases in value to be sustained when interest rates and new construction starts return to more normalized levels. 

Ted Eliopoulos, Calpers  recently appointed chief investment officer, changed the fund s real-estate approach in 2011, when he led that group. Since then, the fund has delivered average annual returns of 14% in its real-estate portfolio. But Mr. Eliopoulos acknowledges the recent high returns are unsustainable.

The fund s goals now are to diversify its portfolio risk and generate steady, modest gains, rather than striving for outsize returns with more speculative bets, he said. Likewise, Calpers on Sept. 15 said it would shed its $4 billion investment in hedge funds as part of an effort to simplify its assets and reduce costs.

 Our strategy is to focus on high-quality real estate,  Mr. Eliopoulos said.  We re still on track. 

Other big investors are taking a different course, eschewing pricey blue-chip properties in favor of half-rented office buildings, shopping malls in transition and other riskier ventures that carry lower price tags. A new survey this month from real-estate consultant Hodes Weill & Associates and Cornell University of 231 large investors found that three-quarters said they were moving money into these kinds of properties in search of higher-returns.

 What s largely driving that is fear that valuations may have peaked  for trophy investments, said Douglas Weill, a managing partner at Hodes Weill & Associates.

After the dot-com bust of 2000, Calpers turned to real estate for the double-digit gains it no longer could count on from stocks. That meant putting money into higher-risk real-estate funds that used up to 80% levels of debt while aiming for returns of 20% or more.

With prices rising, the riskier, debt-oriented strategy worked at first. For the fiscal year ending June 2006, the real-estate portfolio returned 35%. But the losses began to mount during the financial crisis.

A symbol of Calpers  crisis-era stumbles can be found in an empty tract just three blocks from its headquarters here in California s capital city.

Before the 2007-2008 bust, Calpers was part of a plan to build twin 53-story condominium towers on a prominent block-long parcel that overlooks the entrance to Sacramento and leads to the state capitol building. But financing for the condos fell through in 2007, and the land is now surrounded by a green fence and pockmarked with trees, brown grass and pilings driven into the ground.

Calpers, which contributed $100 million to the twin-tower plan, hopes the proximity to an established business district and other new projects, including a new basketball arena for the Sacramento Kings, ultimately will attract new development. An external real estate manager, CIM Group, currently is considering various proposals.

 Calpers is committed to realizing the highest and best use of the land,  a spokesman said.

Calpers has raised its target allocation for real estate to 11%, which would require investing another $7 billion in property, based on the fund s nearly $300 billion in total assets.

Mr. Eliopoulos said that as a long-term investor, he is less worried about possible price fluctuations. He added that while Calpers  new real-estate target calls for investing billions of additional dollars in real estate, the guidelines allow him to be a little above or below the target level for flexibility.

So far this year, Calpers has been moving in the opposite direction. The pension fund has been a net seller of $688 million in real estate for 2014, according to Real Capital.

Mr. Eliopoulos said it would be challenging to reach the new target.  We still have a ways to go,  he said.

Markets
Blockchain Lands Biggest Ever Venture Funding Round in Bitcoin Industry; $30 Million Investment Sign Silicon Valley Remains Excited About Bitcoin s Prospects

By Michael J. Casey 
644 words
7 October 2014
10:05
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Bitcoin wallet and data analytics provider Blockchain raised a little over $30 million from venture capital investors, the biggest single funding round yet for a digital-currency firm and a sign that Silicon Valley remains excited about bitcoin s prospects even as its price has fallen far from its highs of last year.

The investment was led by Lightspeed Venture Partners and Wicklow Capital, with contributions from others, including Virgin Group Chairman Richard Branson and Mosaic Ventures.

Blockchain President Peter Smith said the company intends in part to use the funds to ramp up its marketing effort to a broader community of customers beyond the bitcoin enthusiasts who have so far signed up in for its digital wallet. With 2.3 million unique wallet owners, Blockchain s service for sending and receiving bitcoin is now the most widely used in the world.

 We have captured a lot of the low-hanging fruit in terms of users and it is time to start going after people who are going to use bitcoin not because it s interesting to them but because it s useful,  Mr. Smith said.

He said there is special interest in expanding outside the U.S., including in developing markets, where the company has seen steady growth this year. Emerging markets are seen as one of the key growth areas for bitcoin because the digital currency, which is managed by a software algorithm over a decentralized network of computers and isn't controlled by any government or centralized authority, could allow billions of people currently excluded from banking systems to directly send and receive money electronically. That could lower the cost of commerce for such people, which have had to rely on expensive remittance services, wire transfers and other money transmitters to shift funds remotely.

London-based Blockchain s wallet service differs from others in the bitcoin industry, including from its primary competitor Coinbase and newcomers Circle and Xapo, in that it doesn t allow people to purchase or sell bitcoin for fiat currency.

Mr. Smith argued that his firm has broken away from Coinbase in terms of wallet expansion over the past year because it isn t as negatively affected by the declining price, which is down almost 75% from its peak in late November last year.

 When people open a Blockchain wallet, they do so because they want to do something with bitcoin, not because they want to speculate on the price,  Mr. Smith said.

San Francisco-based Coinbase wasn't immediately available for comment.

Also unlike its competitors, Blockchain has stuck to the principle that control of bitcoins which are managed by passwords known as the private key must be held personally by the wallet owner rather than in the custody of the firm.

Blockchain s funding announcement coincides with a $3 million fundraise announcement Tuesday for New York-based SolidX, which provides bitcoin derivatives trading facilities to professional investors such as hedge funds, and generally caps a strong year for venture capital in the digital currency.

Whereas there is significant skepticism as to whether bitcoin can live up to its promise to revolutionize payments, Silicon Valley investors have poured money into the sector. As of mid-2014, there had been $200 million raised in the preceding 12 months, according to a survey by bitcoin news service Coindesk.

Some of the bigger amounts have gone to wallet providers, with Coinbase raising a total of $25 million over the past year, Xapo $40 million and Circle $26 million. Blockchain, which earns much of its revenue from advertising, has been an outlier, opting instead to grow organically. The company pays its employees in bitcoin and manages much of its expenses in the digital currency.

Chinese Insurer to Buy Hilton's Toniest Hotel

By Craig Karmin and Michael Calia 
427 words
7 October 2014
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
22
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Hilton Worldwide Holdings Inc. agreed to sell its most prestigious hotel, Manhattan's Waldorf Astoria, for $1.95 billion to a Chinese insurance company, the hotel operator said Monday.

The sale price is among the highest ever for any hotel and represents the latest sign of intense international demand for luxury hotels and other trophy properties in major global cities. The Waldorf Astoria's price of $1.3 million a room is also among the highest ever paid in the U.S. on a per-room basis.

The acquisition is the first major deal in the U.S. for Anbang Insurance Group Co., which beat out at least two other bidders for the property, according to a person familiar with the matter.

For months, Hilton has been considering its options for the Waldorf Astoria on Park Avenue, including converting hundreds of its nearly 1,500 rooms to condos and reinvesting the proceeds into the hotel. In recent weeks, Hilton was leaning toward an outright sale of the property. But before the hotel company could begin the formal marketing process, Anbang and at least two other groups offered pre-emptive bids around the $2 billion mark, this person said.

Hilton will continue to operate the hotel under a 100-year management contract. The company plans to use the proceeds of the sale to acquire other properties, in part to avoid a hefty tax charge on the transaction. It is unclear if Anbang plans to convert any of the rooms to private residences, but the insurer is expected to invest in upgrading the 1931 property.

The New York location is the flagship of Hilton's Waldorf Astoria luxury chain, which has grown fivefold since 2007 to include locations in cities such as Beijing, Dubai, Chicago and Amsterdam.

The deal comes as Hilton is enjoying strong growth as business picks up. In August, the company raised its forecast for the year after results for the second quarter topped expectations.

Hilton's shares have fared well since the stock made its debut in December with a $21.30 opening price. The stock has risen 9.3% so far this year through Friday's close, and was hovering around the flat line in midday trading Monday.

International buyers purchased more than $46 billion of U.S. commercial property in 2013, an increase from $35 billion in 2012 and the most since 2007, according to data from Real Capital Analytics Inc.
