No Credit: Small-Business Lending Stuck in the Slow Lane

By Ruth Simon and Angus Loten 
1,307 words
18 August 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

CARROLLTON, Ga. -- Brandi Shirey wants to borrow at least $20,000 to expand the birthday- and wedding-cake business she started four years ago after leaving her job as a bookkeeper.

Demand for the cakes, which sell for $150 to $500, overwhelms her home kitchen. She plans to use about $2,000 from her savings to move into a nearby storefront next month. But the 28-year-old Ms. Shirey believes her credit record and financial paperwork have to be bulletproof before she dares approach a bank for a loan. "It's time to grow," she says, but things "aren't what they used to be."

Across the U.S., small-business lending has been stuck in a slow, grinding recovery behind most other types of business and consumer loans. At the end of the first quarter, banks held $585 billion in loans to small businesses, up 1% from last September but still 18% less than the peak of $711 billion in 2008, according to the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp.

The number of loans for $1 million or less held by banks is down about 14% to 23.5 million since 2008. In nearly one-third of all U.S. counties, small-business lending remains below 2005 levels, estimates PayNet Inc., a Skokie, Ill., tracker of loans by banks, corporations and alternative lenders such as finance companies.

In contrast, loans to businesses of all sizes totaled $2.48 trillion as of March 31, up 9% since 2008. Federal Reserve data show that overall loans and leases grew in the second quarter at the highest quarterly rate since the end of the financial crisis, a sign of improvement in the economy. The latest official survey of senior loan officers showed that banks have loosened standards more quickly for medium and large companies than for small ones.

Some analysts are encouraged by recent signs of a lending uptick by small banks, which often focus on small businesses. In July, the total amount of commercial and industrial loans held by small U.S. banks rose 11% from a year earlier, according to Moody'sAnalytics. July was the fourth straight month with a double-digit percentage increase, Moody's says.

In Carrollton, which has a population of 24,958, and surrounding Carroll County, though, entrepreneurs like Ms. Shirey still are wrestling with the impact of six bank failures since 2007. Those banks held more than half of deposits in the county.

Loan originations in a six-county area that includes Carroll County are 40% below prerecession levels, according to PayNet. Just 13 local businesses got loans backed by the Small Business Administration in the fiscal year that ended last September, down from 25 in fiscal 2007.

"The ripple effects" of the local housing bust and bank failures have "lasted longer than most anybody expected," says William Smith, chairman of the economics department at the University of West Georgia, based in Carrollton. Kathy Yates, area manager of Southern Co.'s Georgia Power unit, says weeks can go by "without a single installation" of a new electric meter at a home or business.

Carroll County Chamber of Commerce President Daniel Jackson says the local economy has improved and lending policies are "loosening a bit, but we still have to look at alternative funding for our businesses." The biggest employers in the roughly 500-square-mile county include the University of West Georgia, Tanner Medical Center and Southwire Co., a closely held maker of wire and cable with annual sales of about $5 billion.

Efforts by Carroll County leaders to spur job growth have helped generate an estimated $655 million in capital investments since 2010.

For decades, local business owners chewed over business plans with bank executives at ballgames and local restaurants, building relationships that help win loan approvals.

Following the bank failures and a push by many surviving banks to rein in and simplify their loan underwriting, "relationship lending is gone," says Todd Anduze, director of the government-funded Small Business Development Center in Carrollton, which advises small firms on financing and business planning. "Now, if you don't fit into their box, you're not getting that loan."

A study of lending cutbacks during the financial crisis concluded that employment and business formation were depressed in counties with more exposure to banks that cut their small-business lending, according to University of Chicago economist Michael Greenstone, a co-author of the study.

Bank failures hurt local economic activity for years, according to a study by Federal Reserve Bank of New York economist Adam Ashcraft, who examined the impact of some bank closings during the savings-and-loan crisis.

Tano Phommasith, owner of a popular Carrollton restaurant called Little Hawaiian, borrowed $650,000 from First National Bank of Georgia in 2008 to move to a bigger location closer to the main, cobblestone-lined square.

Getting the loan wasn't easy, even though his banker was a regular customer. The loan was secured by Mr. Phommasith's home and his parents' home, then worth about 30% more than the loan amount.

After First National Bank of Georgia failed in 2010, the buyer boosted the interest rate to 8.25% from 7.7%, making the loan unaffordable, Mr. Phommasith says. He now pays 5.5% on a loan from a unit of United Community Banks Inc., of Blairsville, Ga., but keeps looking for a better deal. Bank of America Corp., Wells Fargo & Co. and other large banks have turned him down.

"You would think any banker who came in for lunch and saw how busy we are would want to work with us," he says. United Community, Bank of America and Wells Fargo say they don't comment on individual customers.

Some business owners have dug into their savings or retirement plans, turned to family and friends for funding or sought out costlier forms of financing. Biz2Credit Inc. says nearly three-quarters of Georgia business owners who approach the online small-business lending marketplace wind up getting loans from high-cost, short-term lenders. That is the highest percentage of any U.S. state.

Many of those borrowers have good credit and more than two years of business history, traditionally good enough to qualify them for bank financing, says Biz2Credit co-founder Rohit Arora. "The failure of so many small banks there has created a credit desert," he says.

Some entrepreneurs are reluctant to take on debt even though their business is strong. They still feel rattled by the recession, unsure about the economic recovery's strength and chastened by the trouble other small-business owners have keeping up with loan payments. About 3.3% of small-business loans in Carroll and surrounding counties are 31 to 180 days past due, based on dollars outstanding, PayNet estimates. The U.S. average is 1.5%.

"You become aware of every single penny," says Alicia Michael, owner of Carrollton Collision Center, an auto-body shop along the main highway here. She has six full-time employees but mows her own lawn on weekends to keep expenses down. "I don't have a line of credit, and I don't want one," she says. "We run everything pay-as-you-go. I guess we're all a bit gun-shy these days."

April Harris, the owner of a men's clothing store, says she divided her business accounts -- including payroll, loans and other services -- among five banks after Bank of America withdrew her credit line in 2008. Since the crisis, Ms. Harris has required wholesale customers to pay upfront. "I'm sure we would grow faster by extending credit, but that is the reality," she says.

---

Tom McGinty contributed to this article.

U.S. News: Americans Borrow for Cars, Less So for Homes

By Alan Zibel 
692 words
15 August 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Americans displayed a selective willingness to borrow money during the spring, taking out new auto loans at the fastest pace in nearly eight years while fresh home loans tumbled to the lowest level since 2000.

Total outstanding household debt -- including mortgages, credit cards, auto loans, student loans and home-equity lines -- sank $18 billion between April and June to $11.63 trillion, according to a report released Thursday by the Federal Reserve Bank of New York. It marked the first decline after three quarters of increases.

According to the report, student-loan balances increased $7 billion, bringing the nation's student-loan tab to $1.12 trillion. Credit-card debt outstanding rose by $10 billion to $669 billion, slightly below year-ago levels.

The trends suggest Americans continue to recover from the recession by paring existing debt and taking on new loans judiciously. Defaults are generally at low levels, with the share of Americans' debt that was seriously overdue falling to 4.5%, the lowest level since the start of 2008.

Amid this healthier lending climate, "borrowers and lenders are increasingly more willing to lend and take on more credit," said Mark Zandi, chief economist with Moody'sAnalytics.

Auto lenders made $101 billion in new loans in the second quarter, the highest since the third quarter of 2006. Total auto-loan balances grew by $30 billion to $905 billion.

Auto lending didn't experience the high levels of defaults that led to the foreclosure crisis. With auto lending rising for 13 straight quarters, analysts and consumer groups have been growing increasingly worried that loose lending guidelines could lead to a surge of defaults.

Growth in auto loans has been most pronounced among riskier borrowers in recent years, with new loans to borrowers with low credit scores doubling since 2009, New York Fed officials found in an analysis of lending data.

The resurgence in subprime auto lending after a sharp crisis-era decline is most prominent among auto-finance companies, typically owned by car makers, rather than banks or credit unions, New York Fed officials said.

Auto lenders, however, say that concerns are overblown and that defaults in the auto-loan business remain low.

"The proof is in the pudding," said Bill Himpler, executive vice president of the American Financial Services Association, which represents the industry. "The loans are performing and the securities are performing."

By contrast, the amount of new mortgage loans extended in the quarter fell to $286 billion, the lowest level since 2000, and half the $589 billion in the second quarter of 2013.

Total mortgage debt outstanding fell by $69 billion from the prior quarter to $8.09 trillion. Home-equity lines of credit fell by $5 billion on a quarterly basis to $521 billion.

Those who have been able to obtain home loans are enjoying historically low rates, and some are able to tap home equity to stabilize their finances.

Donald Beckwith, 58 years old, and his wife Laurene, 59, are now borrowing $150,000 against the Seattle condominium they purchased in 2011 for $220,000 in cash.

The home is now valued at more than $300,000, and they are using their built-up equity to pay off nearly $80,000 in debt, including credit cards, medical bills and unpaid tax bills "We'll have a little bit more control of both our present and our future," Mr. Beckwith said.

Still, many Americans are still playing it relatively safe when it comes to using credit, a force that could keep a damper on consumer spending.

Tanya LaPrad, a 43-year-old cardiovascular technician and yoga instructor from Monroe, Mich., worked with nonprofit credit counseling firm GreenPath Debt Solutions to pay back $21,000 in credit-card debt over four years. During that time, she also secured a modification on her mortgage to avoid foreclosure.

Ms. LaPrad said she is now far more careful about her finances. "I still have credit cards," she said. "But I'm very, very cautious about how I use them."

The Property Report
Deal of the Week: 'Master' Class on Leasing

By Matt Hudgins 
698 words
13 August 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

PEARLAND, Texas -- The medical-office sector is undergoing a broad transformation with the rise of master leases.

In those deals, a large company or organization leases several floors or an entire building, then typically sublets the space to smaller users. Until recently, the sector had relied on myriad small leases backed only by the guarantees of individual physicians and small practices.

Just this summer, construction kicked off on a proposed medical-office building south of Houston after hospital operator Memorial Hermann Health System agreed to lease more than one-third of the building. HCP Inc., a developer, is building the project on Memorial Hermann's campus here, where the hospital system already has a master lease for an existing 80,000-square-foot office building.

Marshall Heins, Memorial Hermann's head of facilities, declined to disclose the terms of the lease or the rent it will pay for the new space. In general, he says, rents are climbing rapidly and property values are eclipsing those of conventional office spaces.

"Medical-office lease rates continue to rise across the country," he said. "This is probably the hottest sector right now."

The deal is an example of a side effect of health-care overhaul some might not have expected. Across the nation, the value of medical-office space is skyrocketing as the size and credit quality of tenants increase.

In the past, many medical offices were leased by individual doctors and small practices rather than by large, well-funded institutions. In an exodus that began in 2009 and accelerated under the Affordable Care Act, many physicians are abandoning the independent-practice model to join large systems that are better able to create efficiencies and control costs.

For example, the Affordable Care Act requires health-care providers to maintain digital medical records by 2015 or lose progressively larger percentages of Medicare reimbursements each year. At Memorial Hermann, doctors to whom the company leases space will have access to Memorial Hermann's digital-medical-records infrastructure, as well as optional services in areas such as reception, billing and office management. Those doctors, in turn, are expected to steer patients to the system's hospitals.

Master leases such as the one signed by Memorial Hermann are becoming the norm at medical-office buildings, boosting property values and investor demand for the asset type, according to Eric Johnson. Mr. Johnson runs a group that advises health-care companies at Transwestern. The Houston-based commercial-real-estate firm markets HCP's building for lease and brokered the recent deal with Memorial Hermann.

"The beauty of these master leases is that now we're not doing 2,000-square-foot deals. We're doing 30,000- to 50,000-square-foot deals," Mr. Johnson said. "For a developer, with one lease, you can hit your minimum preleasing requirements required by the lender for construction."

Memorial Hermann previously owned its office buildings and leased directly to tenants. About five years ago, it sold those properties and leased them back in order to free up cash for other purposes. "With health-care reform, we spend our dollars where we get a better bang for the buck," Mr. Heins said.

The new economics of the industry also have led to strong demand from investors who want to own medical-office buildings. Some 822 properties with a collective sales volume exceeding $8.2 billion changed hands during the 12 months that ended June 30, a six-year high, according to New York researcher Real Capital Analytics. Median prices on a 12-month rolling average reached $229.67 per square foot in the past 12 months, the highest since early 2008, Real Capital found.

Asking rents for medical offices average $23.36 per square foot currently in Houston, according to CoStar Group, a researcher. That is up 4.9% from the $22.27 recorded in the fourth quarter of 2010 and exceeds the previous high of $23.08 set in the first quarter of 2009. Yet rent growth alone may not account for the steeper uptick in acquisition prices.

PEARLAND, Texas The medical-office sector is undergoing a broad transformation with the rise of master leases.

In an exodus that began in 2009 and accelerated under the Affordable Care Act, many physicians are abandoning the independent-practice model to join large systems that are better able to create efficiencies and control costs.

Baird & Co. "You went from these lower-value buildings to an asset class that became somewhat sought-after, and credit has a lot to do with that," Mr. Rodgers said. "We are seeing that very same trend, the move to credit, occur within the medical-office field."

Wunderlich Securities Inc. research analyst Craig Kucera argues that, more than credit, rising values reflect low interest rates and potential rent growth as large health systems take up more of the market's rentable space. "These large physician groups are thriving," Mr. Kucera said.

You'll never guess how much BuzzFeed raised from Andreessen Horowitz

By Henry Mance 
504 words
11 August 2014
10:30

Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

1. OK, maybe you will

If you guess $50m. BuzzFeed's new investor is one of Silicon Valley's best-known venture capital firms, Andreessen Horowitz. This is literally what happens when you create an INSANELY DISRUPTIVE news service.

2. There's more

The investment values BuzzFeed at $850m, the New York Times reported.

Or $850,000,000.00.

Or more than three times what Jeff Bezos paid for the Washington Post or nearly half the New York Times Company's own enterprise value.

Let's all salute a start-up founded in 2006, which was reportedly worth less than $200m in January 2012.

3. Orphaned pygmy hippos that are looking directly at you

Yes, every article about BuzzFeed must mention that their LISTS ARE CHAMPAGNE FOR THE EYES.

But honestly, that's cool because the site has also been hiring political reporters and is beefing up its foreign coverage.

With Andreessen Horowitz's money, BuzzFeed wants to add more AMAZING sections that your GRANDMOTHER should have told you about, invest in UNBELIEVABLE videos that will MAKE YOU SPILL (ADVERTISER-NAME) COFFEE ON YOUR SHORTS RIGHT NOW and possibly buy some weird-ass tech companies where GEEK'S ARE COOKING THE FUTURE.

4. Which Buzzfeed executive are you?

If you're lucky, you're upbeat founder and chief executive Jonah Peretti. He just rocked the internet so hard, we can all go home now.

But there are stumbling blocks. BuzzFeed has been accused of copying other's work: last month it fired one of its political editors for plagiarism. And its ad-funded model may not be fail-safe: MailOnline, which reaches 180m unique users compared to BuzzFeed's 150m, has had to hire more and more journalists to generate growth. BuzzFeed currently has 550 employees.

5. How did this MASSIVE SITE become MASSIVE anyway?

BuzzFeed's secret sauce is its grasp of how content spreads online and on mobiles, paying intense attention to subject-matter, headlines and so on.

As Andreessen Horowitz's Chris Dixon put it: "Many of today's great media companies were built on top of emerging technologies. Examples include Time Inc. which was built on color printing, CBS which was built on radio, and Viacom which was built on cable TV... BuzzFeed takes the internet and computer science seriously."

BuzzFeed resembles Andreessen Horowitz's investments in big data start-ups, Facebook and accommodation site AirBnB, than the venture firm's moves into, say, robotics.

So, when Peter Thiel, the venture capitalist who co-founded PayPal, wakes up this morning, we know he'll mutter to himself again: "We wanted flying cars, instead we got 140 characters."

6. Secrets that no one tells you about Buzzfeed

BuzzFeed is famous for lists or 5 or 10 things, but now tend to have more random numbers, in order to appear more credible to readers. That is one of the cutest things they have ever done, ever.

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
'Brutal' rotation squeezes mutual funds

By Chris Flood 
533 words
11 August 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys FNM1
02
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Poor results match worst days of crisis

Managers may be forced into high risk

Actively managed US mutual funds suffered one of their worst periods of performance in the past 10 years in March and April despite market volatility remaining low.

The fear is that disappointing returns could force many active managers to adopt more risky strategies in the second half of 2014 in order to make up for the poor performance.

Managers of large-cap US mutual funds fell below their benchmarks by an average of 175 basis points between the start of March and the end of April, according to MSCI, the index provider.

This poor result was only matched during four other periods of exceptionally weak performance over the past decade, including the worst days of the global financial crisis in 2008 and Europe's sovereign debt crisis in 2012.

Mehmet Bayraktar, head of equity analytics research at MSCI, said: "[The returns] stand out as one of the worst over the past 10 years."

Growth-orientated fund managers endured a particularly tough time. Those benchmarked against the Russell 1000 Growth index underperformed by 290 basis points on average in March and April.

Mr Bayraktar said the worst-performing funds tended to have more exposure to more-volatile high-beta stocks, momentum stocks that had done well over the previous 12 months, and growth-orientated equities.

"Brutal" sector rotation presented huge difficulties for both long-only and hedge fund managers in March and April even though the S&P 500 traded within a narrow 75 point range and equity volatility also remained subdued, according to Nick Nelson, global equity strategist at UBS.

Tech stocks , a momentum trade over the previous six months, registered a sharp correction in March.

"Winners suddenly turned into losers. Any stockpicker, regardless of their exposure by region or asset class, found it extremely difficult to outperform," said Mr Nelson, who added that this would leave many managers "playing catch-up".

The MSCI figures come after research from S&P Dow Jones Indices for the UK found that almost 90 per cent of UK active equity fund managers beat the UK stock market last year.

The performance of UK managers was in marked contrast to their European rivals, where more than 60 per cent underperformed their benchmarks in 2013.

Records over three and five years were also significantly better for UK active managers than for their European counterparts.

Aye Soe, director of global research and design for S&P Dow Jones Indices, said the results for UK fund managers stood out. Almost 68 per cent of French fund managers and 63 per cent of German managers underperformed their benchmarks in 2013.

"However, it would be wrong to assume that UK active managers are the shining stars of the fund management industry," said Ms Soe.

As the five largest companies in the FTSE 100 account for more than a quarter (27.5 per cent) of the market capitalisation of the blue-chip index, it was relatively easy for UK managers to beat the benchmark by tilting their portfolio away from these stocks, she said.

Its results for 2013 showed bookings rose 86 per cent to $14.8m. But operating losses more than doubled to $7.8m with its employee base growing from 90 to 147. The company said it was committed to a hiring spree.

Asked whether the new credit facility would help to settle investors' nerves, Paul Harrison, chief financial officer, said: "This is significant because it diversifies our finances. Roger Phillips, an analyst at Investec, said the deal showed that "a near-term equity capital raise would be a matter of choice, as opposed to necessity. Additionally, both the identity of the lender and terms are impressive."

AIG Posts 13% Profit Gain, Settles Suit

The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
20
English
Copyright  2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Outgoing American International Group Inc. Chief Executive Robert Benmosche left parting gifts for his successor, delivering strong earnings and settling a large piece of crisis-era litigation.

In the last earnings report overseen by Mr. Benmosche, the New York-based insurer on Monday posted a 13% jump in second-quarter profit, aided by solid results in its core operations and a gain from the last of a series of divestitures prompted by its 2008 government bailout.

Separately, AIG agreed to pay $960 million to settle claims that it misled investors about its financial health from 2006 to 2008, in one of the largest settlements of investor litigation stemming from the financial crisis.

AIG shares retreated 1.4% in midday trade on Tuesday after climbing 2.1% late Monday.

Mr. Benmosche, who took over AIG five years ago this month and restructured the company as it repaid its rescue package, plans to retire Sept. 1. He will be succeeded by Peter Hancock, who runs the company's world-wide property-casualty unit.

The proposed class-action settlement, with plaintiffs including the state of Michigan's pension plan, was disclosed in the insurance conglomerate's second-quarter earnings filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission.

The settlement, subject to federal-court approval, aims to close out more than a half-dozen separate class-action complaints filed in the wake of AIG's near collapse in September 2008.

The plaintiffs allege that AIG made statements in news releases, financial filings and elsewhere that hid from investors the extent of the company's subprime-mortgage exposure.

The proposed pact "is one of the largest ever achieved in the absence of a criminal indictment or an SEC enforcement action," said Robert Hoffman, a partner with Philadelphia-based Barrack, Rodos & Bacine, a lead counsel in the case representing the Michigan pension plan.

An AIG spokesman said: "The resolution of this and other legacy financial-crisis-related matters better enables us to focus on AIG's future."

AIG was previously the plaintiff in several other lawsuits in which the insurer claimed Wall Street firms misled it about the risks of subprime mortgages they sold to AIG. The company has recovered more than $2 billion in settlements from those cases.

The insurer reported net income of $3.07 billion, or $2.10 a share, up from $2.73 billion, or $1.84 a share, in the year-earlier quarter. The latest results included a $1.4 billion gain on the sale of its aircraft-leasing unit to AerCap Holdings NV. Revenue fell 13%, to $16.1 billion.

AIG's operating earnings, which exclude realized capital gains and losses from its investment portfolio and other items considered nonrecurring on a quarterly basis, rose 11%, to $1.83 billion.

The results showed improvement in the property-casualty insurance unit, after a sluggish first quarter that had prompted concern among some analysts and investors that the company's turnaround was stalling. Mr. Hancock has stressed higher pricing and an exit from unprofitable lines of business that in previous years were valued for their top-line growth, while also modernizing the company's computer infrastructure to make better use of data analytics.

While net premiums written, an indicator of future revenue that reflects the value of coverage sold in the quarter, declined 1%, to $9.21 billion, in the quarter, the unit posted pretax operating income of $1.36 billion, up 25% from the year-earlier period. The company cited improvement in underwriting results, which it said was partially offset by a decline in net investment income, including lower returns on alternative investments.

AIG's more globally focused property-casualty business saw a dip in catastrophe losses, as disaster costs world-wide came in lower than the year before. AIG's quarterly catastrophe losses were $139 million versus $316 million a year ago.

REVIEW & OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Wreck of Cards

622 words
5 August 2014
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
12
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

A law of political physics is that a regulator will always find something new to justify its existence, whether or not it's needed. Witness the plan by the Financial Industry Regulatory Authority (Finra) to gather account information each month from America's roughly 4,000 brokerage firms.

Known as the Comprehensive Automated Risk Data System (Cards), this experiment in big data is intended to allow "birds-eye view surveillance," said Finra chief Richard Ketchum in May. "CARDS will allow us to collect and manage data from firms in such a way that we can quickly identify trends and product concentrations that are harmful to investors and take swift, responsive action."

Finra is the "self-regulatory organization" that polices the securities industry. Overseen by the U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission, it is considered a state actor by some judges but operates as a private, nonprofit organization of member firms. And those firms, especially small ones, are fretting about new compliance costs and privacy risks.

Finra says it won't require that firms include personally identifiable information such as names and Social Security numbers. But critics worry that details on accounts, assets and trading history -- when combined with other databases -- could reveal identities and might lead to abuse if data end up in the wrong hands.

As for the price tag, Finra plans to produce a cost-benefit analysis with its formal proposal expected in a month or so. If Finra enacts a final rule after a comment period, it will then need the approval of the Securities and Exchange Commission, which must also consider costs and benefits.

Additional compliance expenses are daunting for smaller firms already drowning in the Dodd-Frank regulatory tidal wave that is only now cresting. A little more than half of roughly 400 required rule-makings have been completed. And small brokerages are disappearing. Finra says that from 2004 to 2013 its member firms shrank to 4,146 from 5,191. Another wave on the horizon is the SEC's Consolidated Audit Trail. Approved in 2012 as a response to the 2010 flash crash, it will scoop up much of the trading data that Cards aims to collect, but without as much information on particular accounts.

Finra says the ocean of data will help it spot a problem almost in real time, far earlier than if it showed up during a regular examination. For example, officials believe they will be able to more quickly identify classic "pump-and-dump" schemes in which unscrupulous brokers persuade clients to pile into a thinly-traded stock, driving up the price before selling into this artificial demand. Mr. Ketchum says Cards will "revolutionize" regulation as market cops see activity across all firms in the market.

But the financial crisis showed that more data doesn't guarantee that regulators will know what to do with the information, and it's as likely the data flood will overwhelm Finra. Brokers are already obliged to report complaints to Finra, which says it isn't seeing a spike in fraud cases.

Finra's weakness isn't the lack of data but a failure to analyze or act on it. Bernie Madoff falsified his records and likely would have done the same with Cards reports. Finra examiners saw red flags at Allen Stanford's $7 billion Ponzi scheme but failed to act.

A fair suspicion is that Finra wants this data mainly to have it and decide later how to exploit it. This isn't the proper job of a regulator, and Finra's board ought to intervene to shut down this needless foraging of information on 110 million investor accounts.

Markets
How Tax Inversions Became the Hottest Trend in M&A; Tax Inversions a Major Driver in Cross-Border Deals

By Shayndi Raice 
1,353 words
5 August 2014
16:34
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

In 2010, a group of lawyers from New York and London took a bike trip through the rolling countryside of southern France, and helped set the wheels in motion on the hottest new trend in mergers and acquisitions.

Part holiday, part corporate off-site meeting, the peloton brought together top tax and merger lawyers from Skadden, Arps, Slate, Meagher & Flom LLP. Deal making was in the doldrums, but, in tandem, some of the lawyers figured out a plan that could persuade clients to take the brakes off mergers and acquisitions.

Four years later, so-called tax inversions have been a major driver in cross-border deal making, accounting for 66% of proposed U.S. outbound deals this year, according to Thomson Reuters. That is up from just 1% in 2011. Skadden is at the front of the pack, involved in 78% of inversions by deal value since 2011, according to Thomson Reuters data.

The premise behind inversions is that companies based in one country can benefit significantly by buying firms in another country where taxes are lower, reducing their overall tax rate. There have been 22 such deals since 2011, according to Thomson Reuters. Most have been in the pharmaceutical industry, where overseas sales generate significant income that can't be brought back to the U.S. without suffering a major tax hit. But there also have been inversion deals in the media, consumer and manufacturing sectors. Some of those deals have collapsed, amid disputes over price and political scrutiny.

After their bike-trip revelation, Skadden started presenting the inversion idea to banks such as J.P. Morgan Chase & Co., Deutsche Bank AG and Perella Weinberg Partners, according to people familiar with the matter. Some of Skadden's U.S. partners, including top Washington tax lawyer Paul Oosterhuis, flew to London to attend meetings organized by leading London deal maker Scott Simpson, co-head of the firm's global M&A practice, said people familiar with the matter.

The firm is very familiar with cross-border tax issues. Mr. Oosterhuis has previously testified in front of the Congress about techniques multinational corporations employ to shift taxes overseas, while some of the other lawyers who flew to London had formerly worked for the U.S. government's Internal Revenue Service, said people familiar with the meetings. Mr. Oosterhuis didn't respond to requests for comment.

Banks started pitching to their European clients, positioning inversions as a way to make European companies seem more attractive to U.S. buyers. It took time to catch on.

"I would say that initially the reaction was 'Wow, that's a very cute esoteric point, thanks for showing us but we have a real business to run,'" said one banker.

Now, though, inversions are "on the mind of every executive" said Edward Lemanowicz, a tax partner at law firm Dechert LLP.

Skadden isn't the only advisory firm to benefit from the flurry of inversion deals. Bankers and lawyers across the U.S. and Europe have long known that tax benefits could be achieved through cross-border M&A. Freshfields Bruckhaus Deringer, Davis Polk & Wardwell LLP, Latham & Watkins LLP and Sullivan & Cromwell LLP also are significant players when it comes to legal advice. The leading banks for inversions are Goldman Sachs Group Inc., which has an 88% market share by deal size, followed by Morgan Stanley, J.P. Morgan and Bank of America Corp.

Skadden has advised on many of the biggest deals so far this year, including U.S. drug firm AbbVie Inc.'s $54 billion acquisition of Ireland's Shire PLC and Pfizer Inc.'s $120 billion failed attempt to buy U.K. rival AstraZeneca PLC.

Skadden, founded in New York in 1948, has its roots in M&A and remains a global deal powerhouse. With over 1,600 attorneys, Skadden is one of the biggest U.S. firms, with more than $2 billion in annual revenue, according to American Lawyer, an industry publication.

Collectively, banks will have made close to $1 billion from fees on inversion deals since 2011, according to Thomson Reuters. Thomson Reuters doesn't track legal fees.

While the inversion trend has gathered pace in recent months, the idea isn't entirely new. In 1993, U.S. cosmetics company Helen of Troy became a subsidiary of a Bermuda-based shell corporation, thereby avoiding U.S. taxes.

In 1996, the U.S. government started to try to restrict the benefits to shareholders of U.S. companies moving abroad in an effort to curtail deals done solely for the purposes of evading U.S. taxes. However, inversions continued, so in 2004 the U.S. introduced more rules to try to ensure that acquisitions that resulted in a U.S. company leaving its tax jurisdiction were for real business purposes and not only to escape the reach of the IRS.

The 2004 rule said that if 80% of a company's shareholders are in the U.S., it would be considered a U.S. corporation for tax purposes. U.S. taxes kick in with just 60% of shareholders in the U.S. if the company doesn't have substantial foreign operations.

However, the impact of these rules was limited. From 2004 to 2007 a strong M&A market gave companies plenty of options to pursue deals. Then the financial crisis hit, and deal making slowed sharply.

Over the past 18 months, though, the deal making tide has turned thanks in part to signs of life in the U.S. economy. Another leading driver of cross-border deals, many of which are inversions, is the large cash stockpiles U.S. companies are hoarding overseas.

Bringing the money home would trigger hefty tax bills. U.S. nonfinancial companies held nearly $950 billion of cash overseas at the end of 2013, according to Moody'sAnalytics.

"This is a huge driver for the surge in M&A this year, particularly cross-border M&A that has been dominating the headlines," said Sriram Prakash, global head of M&A insight at Deloitte LLP.

In total, the value of all U.S. outbound deals last year, including withdrawn deals, hit $152.14 billion, according to Thomson Reuters. So far in 2014, $349.37 billion worth of U.S. outbound M&A deals have been announced, including withdrawn deals.

Recently, the political cycle has started to turn against inversions. President Barack Obama's administration has grown more vocal about closing the tax loophole that allows companies to do inversions. For some corporate executives, the changing political climate may mean it is now or never for inversions.

"Whenever there's a chance that something could eliminate the transaction there's a bit of rush to do these transactions," said Jason Kaplan, a tax partner at law firm Hogan Lovells. Mr. Kaplan said inversion deals are also creating a "domino effect."

"It's more competitive than ever," he said.

But some advisers say the inversion trend didn't play out in Europe the way they had anticipated. When Skadden first began pitching, the idea was that European companies could gain some power over their U.S. counterparts by offering something they couldn't get elsewhere: lower taxes. Rather than mergers of equals, however, the trend has leaned toward U.S. firms buying out European targets, leading to a sense of attack by some European corporations.

One banker who helped pitch the idea to clients said he originally thought of it as "a whole new angle for European acquirers in the U.S. In fact, it's been a whole new angle for U.S. companies."

Corrections & Amplifications

So-called tax inversions have been a major driver in cross-border deal making, accounting for 66% of proposed U.S. outbound deals this year, according to Thomson Reuters. An earlier version of this article said tax inversions accounted for 66% of announced deals this year. (Aug. 5, 2014)

Goldman Has a Hedge-Fund Rethink --- Bank Dumps Some Less-Profitable Clients as New Capital Rules Take Their Toll

By Justin Baer and Juliet Chung 
945 words
5 August 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Goldman Sachs Group Inc. is upending the way it does business with hedge funds, jettisoning less-profitable clients and increasing some fees on others as it adapts to new banking rules, people familiar with the matter said.

The bank has told hedge-fund clients that the regulations have forced it to set aside more capital, crimping profits at its prime-brokerage business, which executes and finances the funds' trades.

The changes, which are playing out at many of the world's largest banks, highlight yet another way Wall Street has tried to adapt to the shifting landscape. Banks already have cut proprietary-trading units, sold commodities businesses, shrunk stockpiles of securities used to make markets and scaled back investments in hedge funds and private-equity funds.

Goldman also is pulling the firm's own cash out of its largest internal hedge fund, according to people familiar with the matter, to comply with other new rules limiting how banks invest their money.

Goldman and rivals such as Bank of America Corp. and Deutsche Bank AG are seeking to offset the impact of stiffer capital requirements while delivering the higher returns their shareholders expect. Prime-brokerage units brought in an estimated $13 billion in revenue for the biggest banks last year.

Many banks are trying to squeeze more out of client relationships and pulling back from business that ties up more capital, including financing trades. Some, in addition to Goldman, have even cut off funds that don't bring in enough profits.

"It's the most dramatic thing to ever happen in the business," said Robert Sloan of S3 Partners, a financial-analytics firm for hedge funds. "It's a total redefinition of what's a good customer."

Goldman is urging funds to take back the cash they hold in their accounts, charging clients more to finance trades and imposing monthly fees more often on fund managers for maintaining untapped credit lines with the bank, the people familiar with the matter said. In some cases, Goldman has stopped serving clients that produce little or no returns for the firm, they said.

In recent months, Goldman's prime-brokerage unit has been contacting hedge-fund clients to inform them that it now evaluates their business based on the return on assets it produces for Goldman, according to people familiar with the matter. Historically, banks had valued their clients primarily on the revenue they brought in.

Similar discussions have been taking place in meetings and phone calls across Wall Street, as big banks come to grips with the new international capital requirements. Hedge-fund executives say each of the banks is adapting to the new rules at its own pace.

Banks such as UBS AG and Credit Suisse Group AG have spoken to clients about the effect of capital rules on their prime-brokerage businesses, according to people familiar with the plans. Some firms, including Bank of America and Deutsche Bank, are charging clients more to finance their trades, other people familiar with the matter said.

"Everything is being rendered explicit," said Charles Winkler, chief operating officer of Hudson Bay Capital Management, a roughly $3 billion hedge-fund firm, referring to banks' expectations of the profitability of client relationships. "In the old days, it was much more squishy."

The steps Goldman and other banks have taken have led some funds to reconsider their relationships on Wall Street, opening the door for smaller prime brokers that are more willing to work with smaller funds.

"There is less room at the inn" at the major banks, said Noel Kimmel, global head of prime services at securities firm Cantor Fitzgerald LP. As a result, Mr. Kimmel said, Cantor has seen an increase in existing client activity, as well as discussions with new hedge-fund clients seeking to start relationships. Yet many clients are willing to keep their accounts with the big banks if it means they still will enjoy other benefits, including opportunities to buy shares in initial public offerings, the people said.

"It's a balancing act," said Glenn Schorr, an analyst with ISI Group. "You have a conversation, and maybe there's a way of sharing the higher costs."

Cutting ties with certain hedge funds can help Goldman improve the financial health measures that regulators watch closely. Goldman finance chief Harvey Schwartz last month told analysts the firm undertook a "comprehensive analysis" of its books and "identified opportunities to reduce the balance sheet." The effort helped Goldman increase its ratio of capital to total assets, known as the leverage ratio, to 4.5% from 4.3%, closer to the 5% minimum U.S. regulators have mandated as the minimum by 2018.

In all, Goldman sliced $56 billion of assets during the second quarter, or roughly 6% of its total, Mr. Schwartz said. He said the cuts had only a "de minimis impact on our client franchise and earnings potential" and that the firm's prime-brokerage franchise "feels quite strong."

Banks have allowed hedge funds to keep cash in their prime-brokerage accounts as a convenience for clients. But the cash also adds to banks' total assets, weighing down their leverage ratios.

Goldman and other big banks are pushing to streamline their balance sheets before the end of the third quarter. That is the period the Federal Reserve uses when it runs banks' books through a battery of stress tests that gauge how the banks would perform in a deep recession.

A Fed spokeswoman declined to comment.

VIEW FROM THE TOP
View from the top: Simon Segars, Chief executive, Arm Holdings

By Sally Davies 
630 words
4 August 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
12
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Simon Segars joined Arm Holdings as its 16th employee at the age of 23. Born in Essex, with degrees in electronic engineering and computer science, he arrived at the UK microchip designer just after it had been spun off from Acorn Computers in 1991. On his first day at work, he was greeted by the sight of his boss soldering a computer together for his use. Since March, he has been chief executive of the technology group that is Britain's largest by market capitalisation. Edited highlights of a video interview with FT.com appear below.

You've talked about how Arm's microprocessors can enable the rise of the Internet of Things. What does that mean to you?

The Internet of Things is really the internet of lots of different things. We're going to be embedding intelligence into everything that moves and a lot of things that don't. Being able to gather and process data from all around us is going to enable us to deal with congested cities, to feed a growing middle class and a growing population. Many of our licensees have already built microcontrollers [which] they're able to sell . . . for tens of cents. You can write an application to control it from your smartphone for basically nothing, and you can get access to as much cloud-based computing as you like for next to nothing - [this] is driving innovation everywhere.

Do you think the advance of computing power will lead to artificial intelligence that will see robots becoming as smart as humans, and, if so, when?

Certainly we're going to see more intelligence in more autonomous equipment. There's a lot of talk about how robots are getting more sophisticated, but the human brain is a really complex thing - I think it will be a very long time before computing power can really replicate what humans can do. That said, computing power has been growing at an exponential rate for a long time, and the thing about exponentials is they look flat for a long time and then suddenly go really steeply up.

Some observers think that Moore's law, being the increase or doubling in computing power approximately every 18 months, is coming to an end. Do you agree?

Transistor scaling is getting harder and harder. We're starting to see the approach of the point where you can't scale silicon any more. So people are looking at different materials and three-dimensional structures - how you integrate not just in two dimensions but in three, to get more and more functionality into a chip and provide more and more performance.

The British computer scientist, Tim Berners-Lee, invented the worldwide web - but the world's biggest internet companies are American. Do you think the British tech sector suffers from a lack of ambition?

I don't think so. I think there are a lot of very smart people in the UK, and we have a long history of engineering and innovation. Now, how they end up being deployed on a global basis remains to be seen, but I think we'll still see a lot of innovation coming out of the UK.

[Is there] growing interest from the business community for industrial applications for wearable tech?

Some. I think that exactly how wearables are going to be useful is a bit unclear. I think there will be a lot of healthcare applications. I think medical devices that can track your vital body measures and provide a much clearer view of your state of health to a medical professional are going to become transformational in dealing with long-term chronic healthcare issues.

Sally Davies

NATIONAL NEWS
City hiring slowdown offset by small business

By Brian Groom, Business and Employment Editor 
743 words
4 August 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
03
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

City hiring remained flat last month but strong recruitment by small and midsized companies helped offset a slowdown at investment banks, a recruiter has reported.

Astbury Marsden said 2,000 job vacancies were created in the City in July, down 8 per cent on June and down 7 per cent on the same period last year.

"It is smaller City firms rather than the big investment banks that are driving hiring activity at the moment," said Adam Jackson, associate director.

"While the cost of meeting regulatory capital requirements has cut sales and trading jobs at the investment banks, it is still the compliance and regulatory roles in which hiring has been most active."

Other than compliance, risk and technology staff, hiring at the investment banks was modest, Mr Jackson said. "High frequency and algorithmic trading firms are especially active, taking on technology and infrastructure staff from the investment banks as the banks wind down their proprietary trading activities."

Astbury Marsden said Bank of England proposals to extend the deferral period for the full payment of bonuses , as well as regulatory and public pressure to reduce their overall size, was likely to make smaller and mid-tier firms more attractive to investment bankers.

Mr Jackson said: "Smaller City firms don't have the same restrictions on remuneration that the large investment banks do. For the bigger firms there is intense scrutiny of the rewards for top staff."

A more buoyant picture emerges from a survey by recruiter Robert Half. It found that 48 per cent of financial services companies planned to hire during the second half of the year, up from 37 per cent over the same period in 2013.

But, 97 per cent of executives said it was difficult to find skilled professionals, with compliance the hardest role to fill, followed by finance, data analytics and risk. In accountancy, 45 per cent of finance directors expected to expand their teams, up from 28 per cent a year ago. Phil Sheridan, senior managing director at Robert Half UK, said: "Skills shortages across the UK economy are becoming a major issue, with this deficit putting businesses at increasing risk. Our figures show that financial services and finance and accounting, in particular, are current and future growth areas, with professionals being highly sought after and commanding premium pay."

Employees at FTSE 100 oil and gas companies cost their employer on average more than twice as much as the big UK-listed banks, ac-cording to Banc De Binary, a binary options trading firm. It said oil and gas companies paid 106,660 a year in salary, pensions and bonuses per employee whereas top banks paid 54,608.

"Banking has the glamorous image of being the best- paid industry but in reality UK-listed banks are doing less and less investment banking and are placing more emphasis on lower retail bankers," said Oren Laurent, the company's founder.

Confidence falls as growth expected to weaken

Business confidence has dipped for the first time in two years, reinforcing expectations of a modest slowdown in growth later this year, a survey has found, writes Brian Groom .

Skills shortages are also becoming a bigger problem as employment continues to grow rapidly, according to the Institute of Chartered Accountants in England and Wales and Grant Thornton.

Their business confidence index, based on interviews with 1,000 chartered accountants across the economy, fell to +32.3 compared with +37.3 in the second quarter - still close to an all-time high.

A separate survey by the CBI employers' group reported strong orders and output growth for small and medium-sized manufacturers in the three months to July.

It found that companies were hiring at their fastest rate since at least 1988, but export orders had underperformed, possibly in part because of the strength of sterling.

The findings follow weaker data over the past week for manufacturing growth, consumer confidence and house prices. Most forecasters, including the Bank of England, expect a slower pace of growth in the second half.

The ICAEW and Grant Thornton said they expected the economy to expand by 0.9 per cent in the third quarter but growth could slow in the final three months. Business investment was also expected to slow.

The survey found that employment was likely to increase by 2.5 per cent over the next year.

Europe News: Prices in Euro Zone Fail to Gain Strength

By Paul Hannon 
679 words
1 August 2014
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
6
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

LONDON -- The annual rate of inflation in the euro zone fell further below the European Central Bank's target in July, and to its lowest level since October 2009.

The decline is a setback to the ECB which, in June, launched a series of measures designed to boost growth and start to move the inflation rate back toward its goal of just below 2.0%. It is too soon for those measures to have had an impact, but the further drop in the rate at which consumer prices are increasing underlines the severity of the threat confronting policy makers.

Eurostat said consumer prices were just 0.4% higher than in July 2013, as the inflation slowed from 0.5% in June. The inflation rate has now been below 1.0% for 10 straight months.

Figures released by the European Union's statistics agency also showed that the jobless rate fell to 11.5% in June from 11.6% in May, reaching its lowest level since September 2012. The number of people without work fell by 152,000 during June, leaving 18.4 million unemployed.

That was the largest month-to-month decline in the number of jobless since December 2013, and the second-largest since February 2007.

Economists said the decline in the inflation rate is unlikely to prompt an immediate response from the ECB, which has said it may take as much as a year for the measures announced on June 5 to take full effect. The central bank had expected the inflation rate to remain around its June level of 0.5% for some months to come.

"We have to wait and see what will be the use of these measures," said ECB Vice President Vitor Constancio on Saturday. "We won't introduce new measures until we assess [whether] these measures will be as effective as we expected."

Those measures included a cut in the central bank's main interest rates, as well as a new program of cheap, medium-term loans to banks that are intended to boost their funding of businesses.

"The ECB will likely hold interest rates steady and continue to prepare its new long-term loans targeted at boosting banks' business lending," said Tomas Holinka, an economist at Moody'sAnalytics.

The ECB may take some comfort from the fact that falling energy and food prices were the main reason for the lower inflation rate, while prices for services rose at an unchanged pace, leaving the core inflation rate unchanged at 0.8%.

Low inflation is a particular problem for the euro zone because it makes it more difficult for companies, households and governments in southern Europe to cut their high debts and recover from the currency area's twin banking and fiscal crises.

Figures released on Thursday by Italy's statistics agency showed prices were unchanged from July 2013. Previously released figures showed Spanish consumer prices fell by 0.3% in the 12 months to July, while Germany's rose by 0.8%.

Economists warned that the annual rate of inflation could fall further in August and September as food and energy prices continue to ease, but they expect a gradual pickup thereafter.

There are some signs that after a disappointing second quarter, the euro zone's economy has regained some momentum. A monthly survey by the European Commission published on Wednesday found euro-zone businesses were mostly undaunted by growing tensions between the European Union and Russia over Ukraine's future.

The accelerating decline in unemployment will likely encourage ECB policy makers, since it should boost consumer spending over the coming months, and in turn make it easier for businesses to raise their prices.

Compared with June 2013, the number of people without work is down 783,000. Much of that reduction is due to an improvement in Spain's jobs market, but unemployment has also been falling steadily in Ireland and Portugal, while it has remained low in Germany.

Heard on the Street / Financial Analysis and Commentary

1,150 words
1 August 2014
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
28
English
Copyright  2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Samsung Mutes Payout Calls

It's easy to see why investors in Samsung Electronics are feeling let down.

The Korean electronics giant left its interim dividend unchanged Thursday, despite earlier promises to boost cash returns. Its shares fell 3.7%

Investors have been calling on the company to return more cash, through buybacks or by raising its paltry 1% dividend yield. Samsung has said repeatedly it would increase shareholder returns as a proportion of free cash flow this year, and promised last quarter to give investors "a bit more information" after second-quarter results.

But Robert Yi, head of investor relations, said Thursday that Samsung hasn't settled yet on a shareholder-return plan. "We need a bit more time to reach a decision," he said on a conference call with analysts.

Technically, the company hasn't violated its pledge to raise returns as a percentage of free cash flow. This metric, which measures the operating cash flow left after capital expenditures, fell 16% in the first half from a year earlier. Executives cited the need to make long-term investments in areas like flexible displays and new memory-chip technologies for their caution on payouts.

The company didn't say so, but its weak earnings could be making it even more cautious about promising too much. Net profit fell 19.6% from a year earlier in the second quarter, due to a glut of unsold phones and intense competition from low-end Chinese manufacturers.

Also left unsaid was what role the company's cash pile may play in plans behind the scenes to restructure the broader Samsung conglomerate. Since Samsung patriarch Lee Kun-hee suffered a heart attack in May, his family members appear to have set in motion an elaborate succession involving a reshuffling of the dozens of companies under the Samsung umbrella. Mr. Lee's heirs also need to raise funds to pay an inheritance-tax bill that could reach billions of dollars.

For Samsung Electronics, this makes share buybacks more likely than dividends. Buybacks in Korea don't extinguish shares, so they would help the family consolidate control of the company by adding to its stock of treasury shares in itself. Dividends would benefit family members little because their direct stake in Samsung Electronics is small.

Investors may have to wait awhile for clarity on restructuring plans. More clues will emerge when Cheil Industries, a key family holding vehicle known until recently as Samsung Everland, lists shares early next year.

Meanwhile, no matter how loudly they ask for payouts, ordinary shareholders in Samsung Electronics are far down on the company's list of priorities.

-- Aaron Back

Risk for Bonds as the Fed's

Reserve Starts Cracking

The Federal Reserve's tone has gotten a bit more hawkish. Judging from what the economy is doing, there may be more to come.

In the statement released Wednesday following its two-day meeting, the Fed offered a less qualified assessment of the improvement in the job market than it did when it met last month. That was before the strong June jobs report came out. And whereas last month the Fed said that inflation was running below its 2% target, it acknowledged this time that inflation has "moved somewhat closer" to it.

Indeed, Wednesday's unexpectedly strong report on second-quarter gross domestic product from the Commerce Department showed that the Fed's preferred inflation measure -- the price index for personal-consumption expenditures -- was up 1.6% from a year ago. The first quarter's gain was just 1.1%. The core measure, which excludes food and energy, was only slightly more muted, gaining 1.5% compared with the first quarter's 1.2%. Moreover, inflation's pace picked up, with prices increasing at a 2% annual rate in the second quarter.

As long as wage growth remains low, though, most members of the Fed's rate-setting committee will likely view a move above 2% inflation as transitory. In their view, as the statement put it, "there remains significant underutilization of labor resources." In plain terms, there is still a lot of slack in the job market.

But the GDP report suggested the economy has strengthened to the point that it should be able to sustain the recent pickup in hiring. And with that, growth in wages may finally get a lift.

GDP expanded at a 4% rate in the second quarter, better than the 3% economists foresaw. Some of that strength came from companies building up inventories, for which there likely will be a payback as these get run down.

But the strong GDP number also owed something to a better tone in consumer spending, which should carry over into the third quarter.

Moreover, revised data showed that over the past three years the personal saving rate, measured as a share of after-tax income, has consistently been higher than what the Commerce Department had previously reported. This implies that household balance sheets are in better shape than was thought.

To that, add Fed figures showing banks stepping up lending, and it looks as if spending will be more strongly supported in the quarters to come. And with companies running lean, any uptick in spending should translate into more jobs.

The upshot is that while economists and interest-rate futures markets see the Fed raising its overnight interest-rate target around the middle of next year, the odds of an earlier move are increasing. For the stock market that may, on balance, be a good thing, with the challenge of higher rates offset by the better growth that brought them on. But the bond market may have trying times ahead.

-- Justin Lahart

Overheard

It has gotten a lot more expensive to be too big to fail. A study from Federal Financial Analytics says the quantifiable regulatory costs borne by the six largest U.S. banks have more than doubled since before the financial crisis. The price tag in 2013 was about $70.2 billion, up from $34.7 billion in 2007.

That is counting tightened capital rules, higher deposit-insurance premiums and supervisory assessments as well as interchange-fee restrictions. FFA says other new rules can't be quantified yet.

The bank with the biggest tab: Bank of America, which FFA figures was hit with an extra $8.6 billion in costs and lost profits. The next-largest bill lands at Goldman Sachs, due to its conversion into a bank amid the financial crisis. Surprisingly, J.P. Morgan Chase fares best in FFA's study, saddled with just $4.3 billion in new costs.

FFA doesn't try to quantify the benefits of new regulations. But there is one thing that is certain: They are still far cheaper than the bailout of many of those same big banks during the crisis.

Overheard: Here's How Much Dodd-Frank Costs The Six Biggest Banks In the U.S.

COMPANIES
Insurance price falls pose threat to underwriters; INSURANCE

By Alistair Gray in London 
478 words
30 July 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
14
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Competitive pressures in large parts of the global insurance industry have become so intense that some underwriters risk failing if prices fall further, the head of the sector's largest broker in Europe has warned.

Steep declines in premium levels are no longer merely the downward part of a "normal cycle", said Dominic Burke, chief executive of London-listed Jardine Lloyd Thompson. "If this continues then we may well have failures."

Mr Burke's warning is among the most outspoken yet about the impact of low prices for some lines of "big ticket" specialist corporate insurance, which have dropped to their lowest levels in more than a decade.

His comments come a day after the insurer Hiscox said it would shrink its reinsurance business as it forecast further downward pressure on premiums, while rival Lloyd's insurer, Beazley, said last week that the market was "one of the most difficult in our history".

Competition levels are especially intense in natural disaster reinsurance, for which the levels of capital that back the cover have risen almost two-thirds since the financial crisis to $555bn, according to Aon Benfield Analytics.

Low interest rates have driven yield-hungry capital market investors to " catastrophe bonds " and a relative absence of natural disasters has also pushed prices down.

Insurers have continued to post bumper profits because of the lack of claims , but Mr Burke cautioned that some underwriters could encounter difficulties when a big disaster does strike.

Historically, the industry would expect to generate returns of between 12 and 15 per cent. But the rate at which policies were now being priced implied some insurers were targeting long-term returns on capital of as low as 4 per cent, he added.

Mike Reynolds, JLT's finance director, raised particular concerns about reinsurers covering the risk of big hurricanes in Florida, where prices have fallen especially steeply.

Shares in JLT closed down 1.8 per cent yesterday at 10.37 after the company said it was "cautious over the outlook for the remainder of the year", citing the strong pound as well as pressure on prices.

The wary outlook came even though JLT, the world's fifth-largest insurance broker by sales, boosted first-half pre-tax profits from 85m a year ago to 98m and increased its dividend by 5 per cent to 10.6p.

Joanna Parsons, analyst at Westhouse, said the intermediary had so far been shielded from the worst of the pressures because it garnered about half its sales from fees, less exposed to the falling insurance prices than commissions. JLT has also been taking market share and was growing in areas including employee benefits business, she added. However, the analyst cut her forecast for the insurance broker's annual pre-tax profits by 6 per cent.

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
Fidelity warns on China's bad debt; Fund focus

By Bradley Gerrard 
561 words
28 July 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys FNM1
10
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Fund manager says government must act over non-performing loans, writes Bradley Gerrard

Fidelity's top man in Hong Kong, Dale Nicholls, has become the latest fund manager in China to warn about bad debts in the nation's ballooning economy.

Mr Nicholls, who succeeded Anthony Bolton as manager of Fidelity's China Special Situations investment trust in April, says the Chinese government needs to take action on "non-performing" loans.

He says the number of non-performing loans - where loans are either in default or close to default - is rising and the real level of bad debt is likely to be higher than official figures. This fear is holding markets back, he believes.

"The next 12 months will be pretty interesting. The total social-financing figure tries to factor in all lending and, with some of these [non-performing loans] maturing, what we are hoping to see is some of them getting written off.

"What the market needs is progress."

Most global stock markets have produced gains in the past five years, but the Shanghai Composite index has lost 31 per cent in sterling terms over that time, according to data from FE Analytics.

Mr Nicholls says non-performing loans are estimated to account for 1 per cent of assets for most Chinese banks, but he suspects the "true number is north of that".

Since taking the helm, the manager has further reduced its weighting to Chinese financials - largely because of a successful takeover bid for Wing Hang Bank by Oversea-Chinese Banking Corporation. The deal meant Wing Hang hit Mr Nicholls' valuation target and he divested entirely.

Mr Nicholls has used the proceeds of that sale to add to consumer discretionary plays as well as internet-related stocks, such as Tencent. He is also invested up to the fund's maximum limit for Chinese A-shares, companies listed in mainland stock markets and not Hong Kong.

Elsewhere, Mr Nicholls says he has "ramped up" the use of external companies to verify the corporate governance of businesses the trust is considering investing in.

"We are using third-party resources more," he says. "We have people doing background checks and in many cases asking around and looking for red flags in terms of reputation. We have tried to learn from things that have been issues in the past."

In 2011 Mr Bolton was hit by fraud claims surrounding Sino-Forest , the Chinese timber company, and China Integrated Energy, the energy firm. The Toronto-listed Sino-Forest lost 75 per cent of its share price following the allegations.

Mr Bolton admitted he had underestimated the corporate governance challenge in China but added that he had taken steps to improve his research methods for smaller companies.

At the time, he said: "I knew corporate governance was going to be a challenge but I didn't realise it was that bad. We now do a lot more cross-checking and due diligence."

Mr Bolton was one of the most renowned stockpickers of the 1980s and 1990s while running UK and European equities, and in 2010 he returned from a short retirement to launch the China trust. Following lacklustre returns , he retired again earlier this year, handing over to Mr Nicholls.

Bradley Gerrard is news editor at Investment Adviser

Moody's Profit Rises, but Cautious Tone Prevails

By Timothy W. Martin 
738 words
28 July 2014
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
23
English
Copyright  2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Moody's Corp.'s second-quarter earnings climbed 42% and the firm raised its revenue outlook for its analytics and ratings businesses, but executives issued a cautious outlook.

The New York-based firm left its annual earnings targets unchanged at $3.90 to $4 a share. Moody's executives had previously said they expected the second half of 2014 to be stronger than the first six months of the year.

Linda Huber, Moody's chief financial officer, said on the company's earnings call on Friday that second-quarter revenue growth was fueled by structured-finance deals and speculative-grade corporate bonds that have boomed while interest rates are low. But she said it is tough to project that same pace continuing for the rest of the year.

"We had a white-hot second quarter, and we are cautious as we are usually cautious about whether that pace can continue," Ms. Huber said.

Moody's, owner of the second-largest credit rater by revenue, posted earnings of $319.2 million, or $1.48 a share, up from $225.5 million, or $1 a share, a year earlier. Revenue improved 16%, to $873.5 million. The consensus forecast of analysts polled by Thomson Reuters predicted earnings of $1.01 a share and revenue of $803 million. Moody's shares hit an all-time high of $95 in intraday trading, before pulling back to close down 86 cents, or 0.9%, to $91.62.

After a slow start to the year, bond issuance has bounced back in recent months. Credit-ratings firms saw their businesses fall off after the financial crisis, but the downtrend has reversed with the return of strong demand for corporate debt and increased bond issuance in emerging markets.

Moody's said it expects full-year revenue to increase in the low double-digit percentages versus a prior forecast in the high single digits. Much of that view is shaped by stronger activity for its ratings business.

Some analysts pointed to several factors explaining why the company's bullish tone on revenue might not boost profits.

In June, Moody's announced two deals: the acquisition of WebEquity Solutions LLC, an analytics firm, and an increase of its equity stake in India-based rater ICRA Ltd. This month, Moody's raised $750 million in debt. The firm is also adding employees in several divisions, increasing 10% to about 9,500 workers, excluding the new acquisitions, Ms. Huber said.

William Bird, an analyst with FBR Capital Markets & Co., pointed to the deal costs, higher employee compensation and the July debt issuance, which subtract about four cents from per-share earnings.

"It appears that revenue upside will be eaten up by higher costs," Mr. Bird said in a Friday note to investors. Moody's executives said they would revisit their full-year outlook at its investor day on Sept. 30. The debt markets, which have gotten off to a slower July after a strong June, have some uncertainties, said Moody's Chief Executive Officer Raymond McDaniel. "What we're seeing from a geopolitical standpoint breeds caution," Mr. McDaniel said.

The company's Moody's Investors Service debt-rating operation, its biggest revenue contributor, posted a 16% increase in revenue, to $621.7 million. The biggest debt-rating segment was global corporate finance, in which revenue rose 22%, to $320.9 million.

Moody's and other credit-ratings firms have also seen an increase in European bond markets, where bank lending has tightened. They are also expanding into emerging markets.

Operating expenses rose 14%, to $461.8 million, compared with the prior-year period. Moody's attributed that increase to higher compensation, deal costs and real-estate expenses tied to more employees.

For the year, Moody's raised its projections on operating expenses to the high-single-digit percentages, up from midsingle digits.

In recent years, the company has seen a higher percentage of its revenue come from its Moody'sAnalytics division, which helps financial institutions manage risk and sells additional research from its ratings analysts.

Much of that unit generates revenue on a recurring or subscription basis, meaning it is less vulnerable to a slowdown in bond issuance. Revenue for the analytics business climbed 15%, to $251.8 million, in the quarter.
