The Property Report
New Lenders Enter Market and Think Small --- Loans for Properties Valued at Under $10 Million Are Seen as Less Risky as Economy Improves; 'Starting to Percolate'

By Al Yoon 
787 words
23 July 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

A new group of lenders has begun to target properties valued at $10 million and less, and that is good news for mom-and-pop landlords and small-business owners.

Lenders, including ones controlled by asset managers Waterfall Asset Management, Guggenheim Partners LLC and Sabal Financial Group LLC, have launched new programs this year focusing on loans to smaller properties. Many banks and other lenders have been avoiding smaller deals since the financial crisis because they can be riskier than larger properties, and the underwriting costs are high relative to their size.

But now lenders are paying them more attention as the economy improves and loans are seen as less risky. Also, with less competition among lenders for these deals, interest rates and potential profit can be higher.

After sputtering for years, the commercial-property market for small properties is "starting to percolate," said Randy Fuchs, principal of Boxwood Means Inc., a commercial-property analytics firm.

One beneficiary of this trend is BKM Capital Partners, an Irvine, Calif.-based investment company that turned to ReadyCap Commercial LLC for a $4.6 million loan this year to buy Hayden Island Business Park, a Portland, Ore., industrial property. ReadyCap is a unit of Sutherland Asset Management Corp., a real-estate investment trust managed by Waterfall.

Brett Turner, director of acquisitions for BKM, said ReadyCap was able to offer terms that banks wouldn't, such as limiting lender recourse in case of default.

ReadyCap expects to quintuple volume to $1.25 billion by 2016 by making real-estate loans to investors and small businesses under a U.S. Small Business Administration program, said Tom Capasse, chief executive of Sutherland. The loans are "for the small manufacturer in the Midwest or an eight-unit, multifamily owner in Encino who has been left behind," he said.

Lenders made $176 billion in loans of $5 million or less last year, a 75% rise from 2010, Boxwood said. The pace has slowed to $36 billion in the first three months of this year, but some of the newer entrants expect volume will increase as they address pockets that haven't been able to meet still-tight bank-lending guidelines.

While large deals involving trophy properties get most of the headlines, many real-estate markets depend just as much on debt financing being available for the buying, selling and refinancing of stand-alone doctor offices and restaurants, and cheaper apartment complexes. Loans on small or less-expensive properties represent about 15% of the $3.2 trillion in commercial-real-estate debt outstanding, according to Mr. Capasse's estimates.

Partly because debt financing has been tight, prices of small commercial properties as of April have climbed 7% from their low in January 2012, according to a Boxwood Means index. In comparison, prices for larger properties have gained more than 27% in the same period and 54% from their bottom two years earlier, according to the Moody's/RCA Core Commercial Property Price Index, which covers transactions averaging $22 million.

Before the financial crisis, most loans to smaller properties were made by commercial banks that held the loans or lenders that packaged the loans into securities that were sold to investors. Commercial banks have been ramping up lending since 2010 but have been mindful of stiffer regulations.

Lenders not overseen by bank regulators, such as ReadyCap and Guggenheim, also are returning to smaller loans.

Before 2008, at least a dozen nonbank lenders funded some $26 billion of commercial real-estate debt to small properties by selling asset-backed securities. The asset-backed market for newly originated, smaller commercial real-estate loans has been frozen since the crisis, but will likely be revived with a bond backed by ReadyCap loans in coming months, Mr. Capasse said.

Meanwhile, lenders such as Guggenheim and Wells Fargo & Co., which have focused on larger and more standardized loans for commercial mortgage-backed securities, have made a push into smaller properties. Wells Fargo, which started a program for $1 million to $10 million loans in 2011, said its volume has increased, while Guggenheim launched a similar effort this month.

In the market for larger loans, lenders are taking profits of less than 1% to win deals, down from 2% or 3% two years ago, said Chris Haynes, president of Broadacre Financial, a commercial real-estate finance adviser. Smaller loans can still produce more than 2% profit, though lenders must close more in a short period to make them worthwhile, Mr. Haynes said.

LEX COLUMN
EMC/VMware

326 words
22 July 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
12
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

A company that refers to its collection of assets as a "Federation" is not keen on breaking up. So a showdown between computer storage stalwart EMC and Elliott Management looms. Elliott has amassed a $1bn stake in EMC (2 per cent of the shares). It hopes to convince EMC to separate its fast growing subsidiary, VMware. EMC has a market value of $57bn; $32bn of that stems from its 80 per cent ownership of VMware, the biggest player in "server virtualisation," which allows many programs to run on a single computer.

VMware was listed in 2007 to unlock value. But EMC maintains that, despite the separate listing, EMC and VMware (and a new Big Data subsidiary, Pivotal) are an integrated product set. While VMware's explosive growth has led its shares up 75 per cent since its IPO, they are up only 5 per cent in the past two years. That gives EMC the window to buy out the portion of VMware the public owns.

EMC, like big entrenched technology vendors such as IBM and Cisco, has two problems. First, with their already large client bases, these companies grow at roughly the rate of total corporate IT spending - less than 5 per cent. Since the financial crisis, those budgets have not fully recovered. The second challenge is that new software is coming to market that decreases the need for hardware. EMC's core storage unit is expected to grow just 2 per cent in 2014, according to Credit Suisse.

It would cost $8bn for EMC to buy out the public stub of VMware (not including any premium). It could do so using its cash position of $8bn, along with its shares. The risk with Elliott's spin-off idea is that EMC and VMware would lose the benefit of shared arrangements in sales, marketing and other areas. A buyout of VMware would prove how valuable EMC's board thinks those arrangements are.

Real Estate
New Lenders Enter Property Market and Think Small

By Al Yoon

Meanwhile, lenders such as Guggenheim and Wells Fargo & Co., which have focused on larger and more standardized loans for commercial mortgage-backed securities, have made a push into smaller properties. Freedom Mortgage Corp., a residential lender in Mount Laurel, N.J., last year launched a lending division for commercial mortgage-bond loans up to $10 million.

In the market for larger loans, lenders are taking profits of less than 1% to win deals, down from 2% or 3% two years ago, said Chris Haynes, president of Broadacre Financial, a commercial real-estate finance adviser. COMPANIES
AIG's pilot charts post-crisis path; LIFE ASSURANCE

By Tracy Alloway 
752 words
21 July 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed3
22
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

News analysis

New chief faces fresh challenges at helm of the world's biggest insurer, says Tracy Alloway

On the surface, Peter Hancock, the incoming chief executive of the bailed-out mega-insurer AIG, could not be more different from his predecessor, Bob Benmosche.

Where Mr Benmosche is known for his blunt opinions on everything from financial regulation to bathroom fixtures , Mr Hancock has built a reputation as a cerebral thinker with a tendency to choose his words far more carefully.

"We're different people with different ways of thinking . . . but we're much more aligned than perhaps our language would suggest," Mr Hancock, 56, says in his first interview since being named CEO. "We use different words to describe the same thing."

Exceedingly polite, perhaps the worst that could be said about the British-born Mr Hancock is that he has a tendency for "out-there" ideas that sometimes grow to take on a life of their own.

Before joining AIG he was best known for leading the JPMorgan team that invented credit default swaps , the same financial product that caused AIG's near-collapse in 2008.

AIG ended up with a book of derivatives valued at $2.5tn at its height, including $50bn of insurance-like swaps that promised huge payouts to banks and investors should bonds backed by mortgages default.

What followed was chaos: an infusion of $182bn in taxpayer funds in one of the most reviled government rescues of the crisis and a quick succession of CEOs until Mr Benmosche was recruited from MetLife in 2009. The following year Mr Benmosche chose Mr Hancock to help him wind down AIG's remaining portfolio of swaps, and then promoted him to run the company's property-casualty unit soon after.

"In the 95-year history of the company I will be, I think, the seventh CEO, and the first one with a peaceful transition," Mr Hancock says. "This is a continuity decision by the board, which means that there won't be a radical change of direction as a result of the shift."

While Mr Hancock rejects the notion of a complete corporate overhaul, he is tinkering at the edges of AIG's business in typically inventive style.

Much of the post-crisis work has been done by Mr Benmosche, who led the company to its unlikely recovery to repay the bailout - with $25bn of profit for taxpayers.

Yet Mr Hancock faces fresh challenges at the helm of the world's biggest insurer - beyond the obvious but daunting task of managing a company with more than 60,000 employees and boosting the amount of money AIG can make from pricing complex risks in everything from earthquakes to cyber attacks .

For a start, AIG must contend with new oversight from the Federal Reserve after being designated a "systemically important financial institution". The rules mean the insurer is subject to heightened scrutiny of its operations and corporate governance.

Or, as Mr Hancock puts it: "It's meant that the -Federal Reserve, whom we've known well in the past as a creditor, we now have come to know well as a regulator."

When it comes to creative ideas for the company Mr Hancock talks fervently of the opportunities provided by "big data" - the reams of information being produced by an increasingly digitised world.

He will soon hire a new chief technology officer to join a science officer already in place. AIG has a team of more than 130 scientists and technicians who are studying everything from work flow processes in hospitals to road safety in China.

Mr Hancock says the results of this scientific foray come "in the form of advice to our customers, or in the form of giving them price discounts on less risky behaviour".

Famous for breaking down business silo while at JPMorgan, Mr Hancock has already sought to replicate this approach at AIG - combining geographicallydiverse business units in the property-casualty business into single profitreporting entities in an effort to encourage collaboration and the more efficient sharing of risk.

He also plans a company-wide rollout of the concept of risk-adjusted profitability - an accounting approach he introduced in the property-casualty unit to try to better reflect the company's financial health than -conventional "GAAP" -standards. Mr Hancock, a former pilot in the RAF University Air Squadron, appears determined to make the most of what he sees as a lingering post-crisis tailwind.

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
Legg Mason sees value in Europe; Fund focus

By Philip Scott 
549 words
21 July 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys FNM1
09
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Paul Ehrlichman looks past all the negativity to find opportunities, writes Philip Scott

Legg Mason's Paul Ehrlichman is targeting the sectors and regions that are causing the greatest investor unrest, with Europe presently top of his hit list.

Mr Ehrlichman is a Legg Mason stalwart who spent 18 years at the company's Brandywine business, latterly as chief investment officer, before launching the Global Currents Investment Management unit in 2008.

In 2010 Global Currents was moved within Legg Mason's heavyweight ClearBridge business, which runs $94.8bn largely in equity funds, and Mr Ehrlichman isnowheadof ClearBridge's global value equity team.

The team runs $2.5bn, mostly in US-based assets, and offers the Dublin-domiciled Legg Mason ClearBridge Global Equity fund across Europe.

The team also runs the 29m UK-based Legg Mason Global Equity Income fund, which is significantly overweight in continental European shares. Some 40 per cent of the portfolio - compared to a benchmark weighting of 18 per cent - is invested in the region.

The UK represents Mr Ehrlichman's second-biggest regional allocation in the fund but accounts for just 14 per cent in comparison, while a mere 10 per cent is invested in the US.

"Where there is the greatest uncertainty is where we tend to be moving towards. We buy cyclically stressed assets but we buy the highest quality," he says.

While Europe's economy only managed to muster feeble economic growth of 0.2 per cent in the first quarter, he argues the subsequent negativity has been overdone. "We believe a strong possibility exists for earnings to improve given the stability that has developed as the region climbed out of recession in 2013.

"The last quarter was about consolidation and correction, and we believe there is way too much pessimism in terms of growth in the coming years."

Mr Ehrlichman cites automobiles and construction as two such embattled sectors, with German auto firm Daimler, "the poor sister to BMW", an example of the former. "BMW and Audi are still far more expensive and Daimler has been the laggard, but it has a strong domestic demand and it has improved its market share."

Elsewhere, he is finding opportunities in real estate and construction in southern Europe. "We hold some Spanish real estate investment trust including Hispania Activos Inmobiliarios , which invests in cash flow generating assets primarily in residential properties, offices and hotels."

When it comes to companies with the potential to increase their dividend payouts, Mr Ehrlichman highlights the energy sector, where, for example, he counts France's GDF Suez among his top holdings.

Beyond Europe, he believes Japan represents a big opportunity in terms of dividends. "Japan has the best dividend growth in the world right now," he says. "We had been underweight until very recently, but are now neutral."

Legg Mason Global Equity Income has delivered a 16 per cent return versus an IMA Global Equity Income sector average of 8 per cent in the past 12 months. In the past three years it has returned 37 per cent, outperforming the peer group mean by 8 percentage points, according to data from FE Analytics.

Philip Scott is a freelance journalist who writes for Investment Adviser

Markets
Asian Morning Briefing: U.S. Shares Edge Lower

1,884 words
21 July 2014
18:34
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

MARKETS AT A GLANCE

(Data as of approximately 5 p.m. ET)

SNAPSHOT

U.S. stocks declined, but finished well off session lows, in a data-light session as investors displayed caution after Friday's rally. Treasurys finished mixed, with longer-dated bonds getting a boost from geopolitical tensions. The British pound slipped on downbeat U.K. data, while the dollar held steady against the yen and euro. U.S. oil prices ended near a three-week high on supply woes. Gold prices rose on tensions surrounding the downed Malaysian plane in Ukraine.

OPENING CALL

As Japanese investors return to markets Tuesday following a holiday, they'll face a raft of data starting with the All Industry Activity Index for May, which is seen rising modestly after a 4.3% decline in April. Meanwhile, the New Zealand and Australian foreign-exchange markets "will follow central bankers' leads" Tuesday, says ANZ Bank. An annual address by RBA Governor Stevens and a speech on prudential policy by RBNZ Deputy Governor Spencer "both have potential to upset the equilibrium," the firm says.

EQUITIES

U.S. stocks declined as investors hit the pause button after Friday's sharp gains.

Traders said that investors were taking stock after the previous sessions rally, rather than selling on fundamental worries about military action in Ukraine or the Middle East. Both the Dow industrials and the S&P 500 rose last week, underscoring the stock market's resilience in the face of rising geopolitical tensions.

"The buying was very ferocious on Friday," said Jeff Yu, head of single-stock derivatives trading at UBS. "A lot of this is giveback."

A handful of negative headlines weighed on consumer stocks. McDonald's fell 1.5% and Yum Brands 4.3% after the fast-food chains suspended purchases in China, on allegations a meat supplier sold expired chicken and beef to restaurants. McDonald's reports earnings on Tuesday before the market's open.

Toy maker Hasbro and theme park operator Six Flags Entertainment declined 2.7% and 4.1%, respectively, after missing analyst forecasts for second-quarter sales.

Small shares were hit as well, with the Russell 2000 index of small-capitalization stocks down 0.4%.

Broadly, though, U.S. stocks have been notably stable in recent sessions. Shares have been boosted by improving economic data, an accommodative Federal Reserve and persistently low interest rates.

"Investors want stocks, and they're using any kind of slight selloff as a reason to increase their exposure," said Sam Stovall, U.S. equity strategist with S&P Capital IQ. His clients are saying, "Where else do I go?" he said.

Much of the week's attention among investors is expected to turn to second-quarter earnings. Many investors say the current earnings season will be a key factor that determines whether recent stock-market gains can continue. Twelve Dow industrials components and 146 S&P 500 companies are set to post earnings this week, including Apple, Microsoft and Ford Motor.

"This is peak earnings season week," said Doug Cote, chief market strategist at Voya Investment Management. "I'm expecting continued good news. My expectation is that we're going to handily beat consensus earnings."

Among other stock movers, shares of EMC rose 5%. The hedge fund Elliott Capital Management has taken a more than $1 billion stake in the data-storage company and is planning to push for a breakup of the company, The Wall Street Journal reported.

Allergan outlined plans to cut 13% of its workforce, part of a restructuring plan intended to save $475 million next year as the Botox maker continues to try to stave off Valeant Pharmaceuticals International. Allergan shares gained 2.2%.

In Asian trading Monday, Australian stocks closed at a six-year high and Indonesian stocks made gains ahead of the results of the Indonesia election. In China, stocks edged lower. Japan's Nikkei was closed for a public holiday.

FOREX

The British pound edged lower against the dollar on negative economic data in the U.K., slowly pulling back from almost six-year-highs versus the U.S. currency, on a day where investors mostly remained on the sidelines warily regarding geopolitical tensions in eastern Ukraine and Gaza.

The pound slid against the dollar after the U.K. recorded its first drop in consumer confidence in 2014 and, in a separate report, British home prices fell.

"The U.K. saw its first decline in consumer morale this year; that's dragging down sterling a tad," said Joe Manimbo, senior market analyst at Western Union. "The U.K. has seen more mixed data, of late. It hasn't added materially for the case for an earlier rate hike by [Bank of England]."

Investors made few large bets during the Americas session as an absence of economic indicators on the day shifted their attention to the investigation of the downed Malaysia Airlines passenger jet in eastern Ukraine, as well as to developments in Israel's ground invasion of the Gaza Strip.

Investors searched for any signals that geopolitical risks were spreading and would eventually lead to a slowing of global growth. They mostly avoided equities and other risky assets, such as the Australian and New Zealand dollars.

The greenback was flat versus the yen, while the euro traded steady against the dollar.

BONDS

U.S. Treasury prices ended mixed, as shorter-dated notes slipped and longer-dated bonds got a boost from investors cautious about geopolitical risks.

The bond market rallied last week as a Malaysia Airlines jetliner was shot down in Ukraine and Israel launched an offensive at Gaza. Investors and analysts said the geopolitical flare-ups for the moment won't post a big threat to the world's economic growth, but they are keeping an eye on the developments for possible contagions.

"Geopolitical tensions are still driving the Treasury market higher in price...as bombs drop and clashes continue in Gaza," said Tom di Galoma, head of fixed income rates in New York at ED & F Man Capital Markets.

COMMODITIES

U.S. oil futures rose to their highest level in nearly three weeks on expectations that domestic refineries would continue to run at high rates, reducing U.S. oil supplies. Brent, the global oil benchmark, also ended higher.

The price gap between the two benchmark contracts narrowed to its lowest level in months.

U.S. oil supplies have fallen for three straight weeks. Stockpiles typically shrink at this time of year as refineries process more oil into gasoline and other fuels to meet summer-driving demand.

"I think we're reacting to a couple weeks of draws on crude in the U.S., which has been supportive to (the U.S. benchmark contract) in comparison to Brent," said Richard Soultanian, co-president of NUS Consulting Group.

Gold prices rose, as the U.S. intensified its allegations about Russia's possible involvement in the downing of a Malaysian jetliner over Ukraine while European leaders threatened broad new sanctions against Moscow.

Prices for gold have also been lifted by the ongoing conflict in Gaza, as Israel's ground forces waded into the region for the first time in nearly two weeks of fighting, sending casualties higher.

Markets
S&P Reshuffles CMBS Ratings Group; Group Has Struggled to Restore Investor Trust Since 2011

By Timothy W. Martin 
659 words
18 July 2014
18:05
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The head of Standard & Poor's Ratings Services' commercial mortgage-backed securities rating group, which has struggled to restore investor trust after a mishandled 2011 rating, is moving to a senior position in Australia while about one-third of the group was dismissed, according to people familiar with the matter.

Peter Eastham, an Australian national who moved from an S&P position in Melbourne to lead the New York-based CMBS team in February 2012, will return home with the move, these people said. Mr. Eastham took over from Barbara Duka, who oversaw S&P's CMBS ratings group in July 2011 when it took the unprecedented step of pulling a preliminary rating on a $1.5 billion issue at the 11th hour.

Other officials overseeing ratings of commercial real estate deals who were let go include Kurt Pollem, Barbara Hoeltz and analyst Brian Snow, according to people familiar with the matter. In total, 16 people were dismissed Friday, about one-third of the CMBS team, these people said.

In a statement, S&P spokesman Ed Sweeney said the company "is realigning its U.S. CMBS team to increase its resources around the country and better leverage its staff on the ground" in Boston, Dallas, Denver and San Francisco. That move includes a reduction in New York-based analysts, Mr. Sweeney said.

The reorganization includes merging S&P's CMBS ratings team with its analytics division, which monitors the performance of a bond over time. The move will "better position us to analyze the financial life cycle of the assets as debt matures and are replaced by new loans," Mr. Sweeney said.

The combined group will be led by Jim Digney and Deegant Pandya, who will serve as S&P's CMBS analytic managers, Mr. Sweeney said.

S&P, which is owned by McGraw Hill Financial Inc., long held a dominant position in grading commercial mortgage-backed securities along with rival, Moody's Investors Service. That started to change after the July 2011 mishap, a move that angered investors and the deal's issuers, Goldman Sachs Group Inc. and Citigroup Inc. After that ordeal, S&P shook up senior management and changed their ratings methodology.

The leadership changes come on the day trade publication Commercial Mortgage Alert released U.S. market-share data for the first half of 2014, showing that S&P had slipped one notch to fifth place in the CMBS ratings market, losing the No. 4 ranking to DBRS Inc., a smaller competitor.

The top three firms were Moody's, Fitch Ratings and Kroll Bond Rating Agency Inc., according to CMA. Commercial real estate deals are often rated by multiple firms, and market share is measured by the total dollar value of the deals that one company is involved in.

S&P's market share fell to 33.9%, rating 21 of 56 total U.S. deals in the first half of 2014, valued at around $13.7 billion, according to CMA. That's a decline from the 52.4% share rating 31 of 61 deals, valued at around $23 billion it held in the first six months of 2013, according to CMA.

The total U.S. issuance for CMBS deals was $40.6 billion for the first six months of 2014 versus $43.9 billion for the same time period in the prior year, CMA said.

S&P has seen some shifts among the top ranks of its ratings units in recent months. Sharif Mahdavian, who had led the U.S. residential mortgage-bond ratings, was moved into a housing research role, industry newsletter Asset-Backed Alert reported last month.

That was followed by the departure earlier this month by Steve Murphy, S&P's national head of public finance, who retired.

MANSION --- Jumbo Jungle: Borrowers in No Rush to Change --- Demand for refinancing may be beginning to dry up among high-end homeowners, thanks to low incentives and shifting rates

By Daniel J. Goldstein 
687 words
18 July 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
M7
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

The refinancing boom in the jumbo market appears to be winding down. The share of jumbo-loan refinances during the first part of 2014 plunged to its lowest level in almost a year, according to mortgage data and analytics company Black Knight Financial Services.

In April, about 5.8% of refinances were jumbos, Black Knight says, down from a two-year peak of close to 9% in December.

The steep drop in jumbo refinancing comes as the overall demand for refinanced mortgages has fallen amid higher interest rates. (Jumbo mortgages exceed $417,000 in most markets, and are more than $625,500 in some expensive markets such as New York.)

From January 2012 to January of this year, jumbo refinancing was a hot market, going from just under 4% of refinance originations to 8.86%, according to Black Knight data, thanks to low rates, improved jumbo-mortgage credit and a surge in home prices, says Kostya Gradushy, Black Knight's manager of research and analytics.

Home prices nationally rose 25% between January 2012 and April of this year, according to the 20-city S&P/Case-Shiller Home Price Index. "The rebound in home prices facilitated long-delayed move-up purchases and cash-out refinancing," said Greg McBride, chief financial analyst for Bankrate.com.

In addition, during those two years, many of the best-qualified jumbo borrowers who had interest-only mortgages opted to refinance at low rates to avoid having the loan reset to a higher payment.

Traditionally, jumbo mortgages had higher rates than conforming loans. This led some refinance borrowers to pay down their loan balance so they could qualify for a conforming mortgage with a lower interest rate.

But since this past fall, the difference between jumbo and conforming mortgage rates has either narrowed or disappeared, removing the "incentive to refinance as soon as the borrower's balance gets below the conforming threshold," says Bankrate's Mr. McBride. For example, the rate in April for a jumbo refinance on average was 4%, compared with a conforming refinance at 4.4%, according to Black Knight.

Qualifying for a jumbo refinance may still be difficult for less-qualified borrowers. Some borrowers who might have easily obtained loans in years past find themselves out of step with today's stricter underwriting standards, said Keith Gumbinger, vice president of HSH.com, a Riverdale, N.J.-based company that tracks mortgage rates.

For Darren Ferlisi, a loan officer with Integrity Home Mortgage Corp. in Frederick, Md., the phone calls from all borrowers seeking refinancing has slowed to a trickle. "Last year I had a few, but many borrowers are content with having rates and payments low due to the adjusted rates being so low," he said.

Black Knight's data also bears this out. Prepayment rates, which generally indicate the strength of the refinancing market, fell to less than 1% of outstanding loans in February, the lowest since 2008 and down from close to 2.5% a month during the height of the 2005 boom. "It definitely seems like borrowers were aware of their loans readjusting and refinanced to take advantage of the lower rates," said Mr. Gradushy. "The pool of borrowers who are looking to refinance is not as big as it was before."

Here are some factors to consider for borrowers looking at refinancing jumbo mortgages.

-- Houses as ATMs again? The slowdown in refinancing could pick back up if housing prices increase their rate of acceleration, says Bankrate's Mr. McBride. "We've only seen the end of people using the home as an ATM because they didn't have the equity to do so," he said.

-- ARMs leading refis? Mortgages that originated in 2008 and 2009 show the highest rates of prepayment, Mr. Gradushy said, indicating that borrowers of 5-year and 7-year adjustable rate mortgages are refinancing just as the mortgage resets.

COMPANIES
Mining industry digs the 'internet of things'

By James Wilson 
858 words
17 July 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
17
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Cutting edge technology is helping to rein in costs and improve productivity, writes James Wilson

Just 0.5km below ground at Chelopech in Bulgaria, a hard-hatted miner jabs his finger at one of the most useful tools aboard his vehicle: a tablet computer.

Nikolay Hristov, general manager of Chelopech, says producing gold and copper here has become more efficient since Dundee Precious Metals, the owner of the mine, installed 45km of fibreoptic cable to build an underground WiFi network.

"It is the next generation of underground mining. It lets us go to the next level of planning and scheduling and drilling activities that need to happen - and the next level of progress and monitoring those tasks," Mr Hristov says, showing how miners using tablets can feed data between the face and a control room on the surface. "It is like taking the lid off the mine and having a complete look at what is going on."

Chelopech's network is one example of how the mining industry is starting to appreciate the benefits that the " internet of things " can bring to the age-old business of scratching minerals out of the ground.

"The internet of things is now in mining too," says Mike Elliott, of EY in Australia. "These were dumb bits of equipment 10 years ago. If the computer in the office fell over, who cared? Now underground mines are full of optical fibre and bits of equipment that talk to each other."

Miners' interest in joining the data dots and connecting their operations comes as the expansionary ambitions of the commodities supercycle have given way to a focus on reining in costs and improving productivity. Easy steps in terms of cost-cutting - laying off staff and contractors and cutting budgets for exploration - have been taken. Now miners are having to work harder to find additional gains.

Grant McCabe, team leader for "next generation mining" at BCG, says: "There is enormous slack in many mining systems. The industry has not been run with manufacturing discipline. Companies know they have to run a lot more productively."

They must also overcome an industry trend of falling ore grades, forcing them to extract and process more waste to recover the metals and minerals they seek. They are having to do all this in more complex locations.

Rachael Bartels, head of the global mining practice at Accenture, says: "They really need to work out how to get more from less."

In some mines, achieving more from less via technology means using unmanned vehicles to run efficiently at low cost. Rio Tinto and BHP Billiton use autonomous trucks in Australian iron ore mines and Rio is investing in driverless trains. Drones - unmanned aerial vehicles - are also starting to be used.

But smarter mining also means using technology to produce data that help miners measure their performance and adjust where they fall short.

"It is not just having automated trucks - it is the degree to which you are creating a connected worker and a connected asset," says Ms Bartels.

Mr Elliott says: "The most substantial contribution to mine productivity may be the ability to deal with big data around the operation - analyse it, process it and react quickly enough to make a difference."

At Chelopech, the reading of data from operations means problems that used to take one or two shifts to solve can be addressed in a fraction of the time.

Or consider the million or so holes that Rio drills each year for blasting in its iron ore mines in Australia. John McGagh, the company's head of innovation, says sensors in drill holes provide "insanely valuable data" that can help Rio understand its ore much more quickly and precisely.

Drilling for samples has always been the way miners have understood the rock they work with - but now "technology allows them to understand the resource in a much more sophisticated way", says Mr McCabe.

At BHP BillitonMike Henry, president for health, safety, marketing and technology, says "downhole" assay (analysis of a metal or ore) techniques being developed are improving the understanding of a mine's geology. "In the old days you'd drill the core, send it away and results would take months to come back," he says. "This allows us to optimise mine planning and increase efficiency."

Ms Bartels says better data on what material is emerging from a mine can also make miners' marketing more effective - an often under-appreciated part of their business. "You can optimise your output from pit to port and beyond," she says.

The technology and data drive is being supported by the industry's new generation of chief executives.

Mark Cutifani, chief executive of Anglo American, who has accused miners of lagging decades behind other heavy industries in its processes, says the mining industry is "on the brink of enabling a step change".

Ms Bartels says there are still questions about the degree to which miners can industrialise and integrate. "Most have good pilot projects but moving it beyond pilot stage and rolling it out across the entire organisation is difficult."

COMPANIES
Peace under fire from Experian investors; SUPPORT SERVICES

By Andy Sharman and David Oakley 
878 words
16 July 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed3
19
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

News analysis

Chairman lands in another shareholder tussle, write Andy Sharman and David Oakley

Sir John Peace is doing a good job of defying his surname.

The serial chairman - a graduate of Sandhurst military academy and a former High Sheriff of Nottinghamshire - is fast making a habit of facing down shareholder rebellions after enduring a bloody annual general meeting season.

First, in May, more than 40 per cent of shareholders in Standard Chartered - which he chairs - voted against the bank's new pay structure . Then, last week, investors in Burberry - also chaired by the City grandee - voted against share awards worth about 20m granted to Christopher Bailey before he took over from Angela Ahrendts as chief executive.

The hostilities look set to resume today as Sir John hosts shareholders of Experian, the FTSE 100 data analytics group, at an annual meeting in the Georgian surrounds of the Merrion Hotel in Dublin.

The Institute of Directors yesterday added its voice to those criticising the appointment of Don Robert, current chief executive, as a replacement for Sir John as chairman. The promotion runs counter to corporate governance guidelines.

"A desire to maintain continuity should not come at the expense of the board's ability to exercise independent oversight," says Oliver Parry, the IoD's corporate governance adviser.

Previously, the Investment Management Association, whose members oversee 4.5tn of assets, and investor advisory group Pirc had expressed concerns over the succession, as well as the level of pay.

Some investors share these fears. One Top 20 shareholder in Experian says: "A chairman who has moved from chief executive will obviously have preconceived ideas that may not benefit investors, whether that is on pay or wider strategy."

Experian, Burberry and StanChart are not the only companies to have faced shareholder discontent. Barclays, AstraZeneca, Pearson (which owns the Financial Times), WPP and HSBC have suffered revolts amid renewed investor agitation .

But concern over Experian's board reshuffle, which is nonetheless expected to be ratified at the annual meeting, has sharpened focus on Sir John and renewed a familiar accusation: that he holds too many jobs.

Despite stepping down at Experian, he remains chairman at Burberry and StanChart - both FTSE 100 companies. He also has a community role as Lord Lieutenant of Nottinghamshire.

While the 2003 Combined Code on corporate governance advised against individuals holding more than one chairmanship, the Financial Reporting Council's current guidelines do not proscribe multiple chairmanships.

The code does, however, say that company boards should not agree to executives taking on more than one non-executive directorship in a FTSE 100 company, "nor the chairmanship of such a company", hinting at the time commitment required.

David Styles, the FRC's director of corporate governance, says that the chairmanship of a FTSE 100 bank is a "demanding post" that could "extend to a full-time position".

Sir John, 65, describes himself as a "workaholic" at the helm of three "successful businesses". He admits the trio of chairmanships is rare, but says it was prompted by a "very unusual set of circumstances". He was founder of the GUS division that went on to become Experian - which he also ran as chief executive - and joined Burberry to lead its flotation in 2002.

"In terms of my time commitment, I would be surprised if you found any one of them say I don't give the right amount," he says.

But some shareholders disagree. One Top 25 investor at Burberry says: "As an investor, it is better to have a chairman who is solely concentrating on one company. Sir John arguably has too many fingers in too many pies."

A Top 10 investor at StanChart adds: "There have been issues over pay and performance at the bank and Sir John seems to have taken his eye off the ball. Is that because he has taken on too much? That is a possibility." Sir John divides opinion at StanChart. While he has the support of some big British institutional investors, who turn to him as an establishment figure, critics have privately expressed frustration at his low-key approach.

Other modern bank chairmen, including Sir Philip Hampton at Royal Bank of Scotland and Douglas Flint of HSBC, treat their roles as full-time jobs.

The fear is that Sir John's hands-off approach has led to corporate governance and policies that run counter to the trends in modern British banking. The StanChart pay report, for instance, proposed single-year targets for a larger proportion of executives' variable pay, when other UK banks are taking a longer-term view of performance.

StanChart says Sir John does not have a fixed time commitment as chairman, although it is "comfortable that he spends as much time as the role needs".

He attended 50 board and committee meetings last year and sits on the governance, nomination and regulatory compliance committees - although, notably, not the remuneration committee.

"If you measure the contribution I make as chairman by the number of board and committee meetings I attend . . . I would be surprised if that was the only measure," he says.

"We must be very careful as a country not to take on a box-ticking mentality."

Tech
Financing Would Give Game Startup $3 Billion Valuation; Machine Zone Gets Pricey Looks for Multiplayer Game With Translator

By Douglas MacMillan and Telis Demos 
595 words
16 July 2014
20:14
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

A little-known game developer called Machine Zone Inc. is on the verge of raising a round of financing that would catapult it into the ranks of the most valuable start-ups in the world.

The developer of "Game of War: Fire Age," a popular mobile game with a built-in language translator, is in advanced talks with J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. about raising funding at a valuation of more than $3 billion, according to people familiar with the negotiations. It was unclear how much money the company is trying to raise.

It got its start in 2008 as a developer of a dating app, but co-founders, led by Chief Executive Gabriel Leydon, shifted their focus to making strategy games for smartphones and changed the company's name to Machine Zone in 2012.

Launched a year ago, "Game of War" is a new twist on the genre of massively-multiplayer online games, such as Activision Blizzard Inc.'s "World of Warcraft," that bring together millions of players to go on collaborative quests or battle each other in a shared virtual world.

One of the unique attributes of "Game of War" is its built-in language translator that lets gamers of different nationalities interact in the same online universe. Whereas most online multiplayer games group together gamers in the same region, "Game of War" lets playerswhether in Brazil, Singapore or New Zealand share in the same quests and battles.

"This is what my goal is, to network the entire world together into one real-time environment," Mr. Leydon said in an interview with tech blog Inside Mobile Apps last August.

Mr. Leydon declined to comment on Wednesday.

The game relies on translation services powered by Google Inc. and Microsoft Corp., and uses a technology called machine learning to build its own dictionary of terms that are more specific to videogames.

Sales of virtual currency within the app have added up.The company predicts revenue this year of around $600 million, according to a person familiar with its business. Apple ranks the game at No. 3 on its list of "top grossing" game apps.

The Palo Alto, Calif., company is following a similar trajectory as Finnish developer Supercell, which was a virtual unknown a few years ago before its combat game "Clash of Clans" took off. In April 2013, Supercell raised $130 million in funding from investors including Institutional Venture Partners at a $770 million valuation. Six months later, the value rose to $3 billion the largest valuation for a mobile app company at the time, according to investment bank Rutberg & Co. when it sold a 51% stake to SoftBank Corp. last year.Machine Zone previously raised only about $13 million from investors including Menlo Ventures, Anthos Capital and Baseline Ventures, according to Dow Jones VentureSource. The company has more than 150 employees, including alumni of top game makers like Electronic Arts Inc. and Activision Blizzard.

By valuing a young startup so highly, investors are betting Machine Zone can produce many more lucrative apps. Game makers such as Zynga Inc., Rovio and King Digital have struggled to follow up their biggest hits with repeat successes.

Investors "like broad diverse portfolios and they don't tend to reward guys that are one-hit wonders," Michael Pachter, a gaming analyst at Wedbush Securities, said.

Zynga is valued at $2.7 billion and King is valued at about $6.4 billion.

GM, VocalIQ Work On Voice Command

By Amir Mizroch and Orr Hirschauge 
545 words
16 July 2014
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
17
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

General Motors Co. is working with U.K.-based software maker VocalIQ Ltd. to develop an advanced voice-control system for vehicles that would allow spoken commands to access navigation and entertainment systems and accessories.

The work is being overseen by GM's Advanced Technical Center in Israel, which focuses on research and development of autonomous vehicles, robotics, and human-machine interface.

The two companies are negotiating a contract, according to two people familiar with the process, although they have been cooperating for several months.

Spun-out in 2011 from the University of Cambridge's Dialogue Systems Group, VocalIQ recently raised GBP 750,000 ($1.3 million) in funding from investors led by Amadeus Capital Partners. VocalIQ, which is still based in Cambridge, develops machine learning software to enable voice control of devices.

GM and VocalIQ have been working on a prototype for a dialogue management system that could be integrated into dashboard navigation systems, the people said. The development would allow drivers to turn on wipers, adjust the volume of its entertainment system, or request directions by spoken commands.

GM now offers several 2014 models with Apple Inc.'s Siri Eyes Free voice system tied to its Chevrolet MyLink infotainment system.

Richard Anton of Amadeus Capital Partners said VocalIQ's technology was a step beyond the currently available voice-recognition products.

"The technology enables the machine to learn how people say different things. In situations like driving a vehicle, you can't afford to spend any time distracting yourself on the road. You need to be able to get the machine to understand what you're trying to say the first time," he said.

VocalIQ Chief Executive Blaise Thomson said: "Our plan is to build a demonstrator application that could be used in automotive and show that the performance really is better using our approach."

He added "We are in negotiations with a large automotive [company]" on the development, but said no contract has been signed.

Gil Golan, director of the GM technical center in Israel, said the company has been working with VocalIQ's chairman, an expert in spoken language processing, for almost three years and with VocalIQ itself over the past year. He said the system being developed has the potential to be used broadly and the auto maker is considering an investment in the firm.

"The dialogue system developed by VocalIQ can be applied to many different industries and we've been tailoring the technology to the car industry and registering many patents along the way," Mr. Golan said.

The dialogue-management system that VocalIQ develops is a software component that sits on top of speech-recognition systems, like those developed by Nuance Communications Inc. and Google Inc.

"The recent launch of OnStar 4G/LTE systems in GM models in North America means that we now have the bandwidth necessary to put the dialogue manager either in the cloud or in the car itself and we are considering both options.

Implementation via the cloud can mean a shorter time to market. Basically, any input that is currently being done via a button can be done through voice potentially," Mr. Golan said.

Lou Eccleston, who led the fast-growing S&P Capital IQ division at McGraw Hill Financial Inc., departed Monday to pursue other opportunities, according to an internal company memo.

Imogen Dillon Hatcher, S&P Capital IQ's chief commercial officer, will take over as acting president, as the company conducts "an internal and external search for a successor," McGraw Hill Chief Executive Douglas Peterson said in a Monday-afternoon memo distributed to employees and obtained by The Wall Street Journal.

Mr. Eccleston became president of S&P Capital IQ in 2010, the second-biggest unit of McGraw Hill by revenue, after its ratings division. S&P Capital IQ, with $1.2 billion in 2013 revenue, specializes in providing investors with real-time data and analytics.

The unit was a standout performer in McGraw Hill's first quarter, with revenue growing 4% to $301 million compared with the prior-year quarter. S&P Capital IQ is divided into three components: desktop and enterprise solutions, credit solutions and markets intelligence.

S&P Capital IQ has unrolled new products over the past year and is competing for business with Thomson Reuters Corp., Bloomberg LP and others.

Credit raters, such as Standard & Poor's and rivals Moody's Corp. and Fitch Ratings, have beefed up their real-time analytics divisions, as investors desire more information following the financial crisis. Those units are among the fastest-growing at all of the rating firms.

Ms. Dillon Hatcher is a newcomer to S&P Capital IQ. She started in her current role in February, after leaving the London Stock Exchange, where she served as executive director of global sales.

Mr. Eccleston joined McGraw Hill in 2009, according to the company memo.

FRONT PAGE - FIRST SECTION
Insurers trawl big data  to find those careful with cash are unlikely to crash

By Alistair Gray, Insurance Correspondent 
493 words
12 July 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed3
01
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

It is the unlikely question that could save your life. Next time a colleague offers you a lift home, ask him if he paid his electricity bill on time.

His insurer will already know the answer. The industry has started quietly trawling customers' finances and other sources of "big data" after proving that those who are careful with their cash have fewer car crashes.

The correlation is so strong that Lloyds Banking Group has begun offering prudent account holders savings of as much as 20 per cent on their car insurance premiums.

Scottish Widows, Lloyds' insurance arm, has not established why consumers who stay within overdraft limits or avoid bounced debit card payments have fewer accidents, but insiders suggest that the thrifty tend to be more responsible in other areas of their life.

The development shows how access to big data is revolutionising the insurance sector. Groups are accessing a wide range of data on everything from financial probity to shopping habits to determine the risk premium for customers.

Tesco, which tracks consumer behaviour through its Clubcard loyalty scheme, offers those it deems less risky based on their shopping habits discounts of as much as 40 per cent on home and car insurance.

Aviva has calculated the optimal distance from the street at which a property is least likely to be burgled: not too secluded nor too exposed. Executives at Aviva's Canadian business have established that houses within a radius of a few hundred metres from cinemas are more likely to be vandalised.

"This is not just a backroom theoretical exercise," said -Maurice Tulloch, who runs Aviva UK's general insurance business.

"If our analyst finds something new on a Monday, that can be live and impacting our prices on the Tuesday," he said.

Simon Douglas, director of AA Insurance, said: "The winners in the insurance market will be the ones that have got the data insights that others don't have. It could be supermarkets, banks or social media companies."

Insurers, including Scottish Widows and Tesco, say that they use such personal data to offer some consumers cheaper policies, not to penalise those they deem riskier.

Yet the big data trend ultimately means some policyholders will pay more than others. Brokers say the main reason flood insurance is increasingly unaffordable for some is not climate change but because insurers know more about the risks posed by particular properties.

Emma Carr, acting director of Big Brother Watch, said of the use of sensitive data by insurers: "Despite this being within the law, the way many companies go about doing this is underhand and goes far beyond what customers would expect them to do with their data."

She called on companies to give consumers the option to opt in to such analysis, rather than merely allowing them to opt out.

Infosys quarterly results beat forecasts

By Avantika Chilkoti in Mumbai 
570 words
11 July 2014
06:48
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Infosys posted first-quarter profit that beat expectations and maintained its sales growth forecast for the year, signalling further improvement at the Indian IT group in spite of a period of unsettling churn in senior management.

Since returning as executive chairman last year, co-founder Narayana Murthy has focused on cutting costs and shifting the focus of India's second-largest IT services exporter by revenue back to its core services, following a mistimed attempt to move up the value chain.

Infosys earned net profit of Rs28.86bn ($480m) in the three months to June, up 21.6 per cent from the previous year, on revenue of Rs127.70bn, which was up 13.3 per cent per cent year-on-year. Analysts at brokerage Kotak Securities had forecast post-tax profit of Rs27.16bn on revenue of Rs128.36bn.

"They've delivered well on the margins front given that they had headwinds of wage hikes, increasing visa costs and the appreciation of the rupee," said Ankita Somani, an analyst at Mumbai-based MSFL Research.

The Indian rupee has gained some 8 per cent against the US dollar since a September collapse in emerging market currencies.

Employee costs have also been an issue, with a high attrition rate of 19.5 per cent in the quarter and an increase in the cost of US visas that analysts say will hit India's IT outsourcing industry more broadly given the large number of projects involving US-based clients.

Infosys also maintained its outlook for sales growth of 7-9 per cent in dollar terms this financial year, suggesting the Bangalore-based group is on the path to recovery as Mr Murthy hands over to new leadership.

Mr Murthy stepped down as executive chairman last month and the company said Vishal Sikka, a former senior executive at SAP, will take over as chief executive in August. Mr Sikka is the first leader not from among the group of seven executives who founded Infosys in 1981.

More than 10 senior executives have left the company since Mr Murthy's return to the helm, creating volatility that - along with a slowdown for some clients in the retail and tech industries - led Infosys to issue a profit warning earlier this year.

Shares in Infosys were up 1.1 per cent on Friday morning in Mumbai at Rp3,330. They have dropped nearly 5 per cent over the past six months, in contrast to a gain of almost 23 per cent for the benchmark Nifty equities index amid political optimism surrounding the election of new prime minister Narendra Modi.

Mr Sikka has built a reputation for innovation in up and coming areas such as data analytics and cloud computing, and observers suggest his appointment signals Infosys' intention to focus itself as a global technology group, moving away from being simply an outsourcing vendor.

Analysts at Mumbai-based brokerage Motilal Oswal foresee a period of change for Infosys, which has so far shunned large acquisitions in spite of a growing cash pile. The company had Rs259.5bn in cash and equivalents at the end of the most recent financial year.

"With Mr Sikka's prowess in technology innovation . . . transformative solutions will not be lacking," Motilal Oswal said in a note to clients. As a results, investments will likely pick up, "increasing the possibility of cash seeing swifter allocation".

U.S. News: Red Flags in Medicare Billing --- Report on Questionable Lab Payments Shows Program's Vulnerability to Abuse

By Janet Adamy 
804 words
9 July 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A3
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Corrections & Amplifications

Medicare allowed $1.5 billion in 2010 payments to clinical laboratories for claims associated with questionable billing. Because of a calculation error by the Department of Health and Human Services' Office of Inspector General, a U.S. News article on July 9 about red flags in Medicare billing incorrectly said that the total was $1.7 billion. Also, the value of duplicate lab tests during that time period was $38.5 million, and claims with problematic physician, lab provider or beneficiary identification numbers totaled $209.6 million, representing at least 20.6% of laboratories analyzed. A chart accompanying the article incorrectly listed those values as $234.6 million, $210.2 million and at least 20.7%, respectively, due to the office's calculation error. A corrected chart is available at WSJ.com/Corrections.

(WSJ August 21, 2014)

(END)

Medicare allowed $1.7 billion in 2010 payments to clinical laboratories for claims that raised red flags, according to a report to be released Wednesday, the latest example of how the federal insurance program for the elderly and disabled is susceptible to misspending and abuse.

The report, by the Department of Health and Human Services' Office of Inspector General, found that more than 1,000 laboratories showed five or more measures of questionable billing during that year, the latest available when the office began compiling the data. That includes metrics signifying higher-than-average billing, using ineligible physician identification numbers and administering duplicate tests, among other things.

The findings show how Medicare remains vulnerable to inappropriate spending. In fiscal 2013, the rate of improper payments in Medicare was 10.1%, representing $36 billion in improper payments, according to the Centers for Medicare and Medicaid Services. That is up from 8.5%, or $29.6 billion, the previous year.

Clinical laboratory services, which include blood counts, cholesterol screenings and urinalyses, lend themselves to suspect billing in part because physicians order the services instead of providing them directly, according to Medicare fraud specialists.

"Wherever you've got a set of multiple parties involved in the care of a given patient, you've got an opportunity for the dishonest to make something of it," said Bill Mahon, president of Mahon Consulting Group in Great Falls, Va., which helps health-care payers reduce their exposure to fraud.

Medicare is the nation's largest payer of clinical laboratory services, paying $8.2 billion in 2010 for lab services in Part B of the program, which covers doctor visits and other clinician services. From 2005 to 2010, Medicare enrollment in Part B rose by 10%, while spending for lab services in that part of the program increased by 29%.

The inspector general's office detailed several examples of questionable billing but didn't release labs' names. It singled out an independent lab in Texas that was allowed to bill an average of $61,434 per ordering physician, or 15 times the average for independent clinical laboratories. All of the lab's claims were for beneficiaries that Medicare flagged as having compromised identification numbers, which are tied to questionable billing activity.

Of the claims billed, 89% were for beneficiaries who lived more than 150 miles from the physician who ordered the test, an indicator of questionable billing. Medicare allowed $2 million in billing for the lab in 2010.

Forty-three percent of the labs that exceeded the threshold for having five or more measures of questionable billing were in California and Florida. Just 13% of all labs were in those states.

At one California lab, all 1,224 beneficiaries for whom the lab submitted claims had compromised beneficiary identification numbers. Additionally, 45% had ineligible ordering-physician identification numbers. The lab was allowed an average of $61,041 in billing per ordering physician, again 15 times the average among independent labs.

The report didn't measure how the 2010 questionable-billing findings compared with previous years, but the inspector general has highlighted fraudulent billing among clinical laboratories. A 2000 inspector-general report found fraud schemes that included billing for services not performed or needed, and a nationwide law-enforcement project on laboratory scams resulted in settlements against several large lab companies.

In a letter to Inspector General Daniel R. Levinson, CMS Administrator Marilyn Tavenner said the agency agreed with his recommendation that the agency direct Medicare contractors to investigate the labs identified as having questionable billing.

The agency said advanced analytics have identified or prevented more than $210 million in improper Medicare payments in the past two years. Aaron Albright, a spokesman for the agency, said CMS "will continue to utilize all available tools to identify and prevent improper billing practices."

Report Raises Red Flags on Medicare Lab Billing; Questionable Payments Shows Health Program's Vulnerability to Abuse

Medicare allowed $1.5 billion in 2010 payments to clinical laboratories for claims that raised red flags, according to a report to be released Wednesday, the latest example of how the federal insurance program for the elderly and disabled is susceptible to misspending and abuse.

COMPANIES
Lynch's Invoke Capital dips into healthcare technology; INVESTMENT COMPANIES

By Murad Ahmed, European Technology Correspondent 
454 words
9 July 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
22
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Mike Lynch, the founder of UK software group Autonomy, has revealed the latest investment from his $1bn technology fund as he forges ahead with deals despite investigations into the sale of his former business.

Invoke Capital is leading a $13.75m investment in Sophia Genetics, a Swiss genetics company. Other investors in the round include Swisscom, the media group, and Endeavour Vision, a Swiss venture capital firm.

The investment is Invoke's first entry into healthcare, which Mr Lynch believes is ripe for disruption by technology groups that can utilise big data.

Sophia Genetics will work with Genalys, a British big data start-up within Invoke's portfolio, to develop a mathematical approach to medicine that can predict problems with a person's health. It works with hospitals and researchers, helping to provide an early diagnosis for patients and optimised treatment of cancer and genetic diseases.

"It's a bit like playing poker when you can see the other guy's hand," said Mr Lynch. "There is no doubt this is the beginning of personalised medicine which will be a revolution in healthcare."

He said the fund is likely to pursue other similar investments. Mr Lynch said he considered 40 genetics companies before settling on Sophia Genetics, believing the group had the greatest chance of making an impact on "an industrial scale".

Invoke seeks to fund young companies involved in "deep technologies" that give them a clear advantage over rivals in their industry.

Previous investments include two Cambridge-based start-ups, Darktrace, a Cambridge-based cyber security company and Neurence, an augmented reality platform.

Mr Lynch remains in the crosshairs of Hewlett-Packard's legal team over Autonomy, which HP acquired for $11bn in 2011. The Silicon Valley company last week said it would continue to press claims against him and Sushovan Hussain, Autonomy's former chief financial officer, over alleged fraud related to acquisition.

HP last week settled shareholder lawsuits over the $8.8bn writedown it subsequently took on the value of Autonomy. It claims that some $5bn of the writedown was due to misrepresentations by former Autonomy executives about the state of the business. Autonomy's management team have always denied any wrongdoing.

Mr Lynch said the issues with HP had not affected his work with Invoke, and had not put start-ups off making deals with Invoke.

"We raised a billion dollars in four weeks, which is much faster than some of the more established funds," said Mr Lynch. "Other than hurling bricks at each other in the press, there is no litigation [with HP]. There's not actually that much of a distraction."
